Development of a slicing and control application for construction­scale 3D printing
with polymeric concrete composites
Master Thesis
August 3, 2020 – January 3, 2021
By
Sander Franciscus Wilhelmus Leusink
s182577
Supervisors:
Holger H. Koss, Associate Professor
Julian Christ, PhD­Candidate

==PAGE:4==
Abstract
The aim of this thesis has been to develop a path generating process that makes uses of
the additional degrees of freedom that polymeric concrete composites (BPC) and multi­
axis robotic arm extrusion provide. All the relevant steps in this process have been demon­
strated.
A review of existing slicing strategies has been made, resulting in an overview of conven­
tional and new slicing strategies, including discrete rotational, continuous rotational and
non­planar slicing methods.
A slicing method has been developed that makes use of a continuous rotational approach,
defining slicing planes based on calculated model skeleton curves. These curves are de­
fined following an initial partitioning phase, in order to achieve correct spline interpolation,
as well as to allow different types of model elements to be sliced differently. These par­
titioning methods and skeleton calculations have been designed for simple, branching,
arch and dome structures, each requiring a distinct approach.
Following the slicing stage, an extensive post­processing stage is included to optimise the
print path, to improve the accuracy of the deposited material and to generate infill patterns
if desired.
The print path is then exported to G­code, which can be visualised and simulated using
existing tools. A custom translation tool has been developed to transform this created
G­code file into KRL files used for the control of a KUKA robotic arm.
Several tests have been done to illustrate the slicing approach and the control of the
robotic arm. These tests are based on models designed after a review of structures result­
ing from biomimicry and topology optimisation. The designed models are subsequently
classified based on their estimated sliceability and buildability.
Further areas of development of the slicing have been discussed and should primarily
look at improvements to the partitioning methods and to skeleton calculations.
Future development of the slicing method should follow after initial tests with the poly­
meric materials. Suggestions for tests have been made and should focus on testing the
accuracy of the print path, as well as on relating the material properties to the slicing
parameters.

are still constantly being made. Currently Additive Manufacturing seems to be one of the
major developments in manufacturing and construction, and I am delighted to have had
the opportunity to contribute to that.

==PAGE:11==
1
Introduction
The buildings and construction industry is responsible for more than a third of global en­
ergy and process­related CO2­emissions, thus having a large impact on the current cli­
mate crisis [3]. Even though the production of cement is relatively energy efficient, it
accounts for up to 10% of anthropogenic global CO2 emissions [4, 5]. This is due to con­
crete being the most widely used construction material worldwide, thanks to its inherently
useful characteristics such as the availability of resource material and practical behaviour
during construction [5]. Furthermore, in the case of construction with concrete in remote
areas where cement is not locally produced, an additional environmental cost is present
due to the required transportation.
In order to reduce this environmental impact, three main directions are available. Firstly,
generally replacing concrete with more eco­friendly building materials will reduce the im­
pact of concrete as a building material. However, many alternatives to concrete do not
provide the same useful characteristics described above. Secondly, developing new bind­
ing materials for the use in concrete that require less energy to produce and/or can be
more locally sourced might allow for environmental gains while retaining the other proper­
ties of concrete. Thirdly, more efficient material use in constructions will allow for reducing
the total amount of material required for structures.
1.1
Biopolymer concrete
One area of developments for the replacement of cement in concrete is biopolymer con­
crete (BPC) as currently researched at DTU [6]. With this, cement is replaced as a binding
material in concrete by polymers sourced from biological material such as proteins and
polysaccharides. These binding materials are mixed with aggregates to achieve promis­
ing material strengths and rheology.
In addition to the potential gains in energy use, BPC is expected to have beneficial prop­
erties for the use in extrusion based additive manufacturing. One of these is the presence
of thermosetting or thermoplastic properties, which allows for better control of the material
during extrusion based additive manufacturing (see section 1.2.2.2) [7].
Furthermore, the binding materials can potentially be produced through simple manufac­
turing processes, allowing for nearer resource utilisation in remote areas [7].
1.2
Digital concrete
Due to technological advances in construction materials, design approaches and manu­
facturing methods, it has become more feasible to transform more optimised digital de­
signs into physical products [8]. Digital concrete (DC) or digital fabrication with concrete
(DFC) allows for improved shape efficiency as structures can be created following the
idea of form follows force [9], leading to potential material reductions. Additionally, new
manufacturing methods could allow for a higher degree of customisation of structures, as
well as optimised and automated construction workflows [8, 10].
1.2.1
Digital fabrication methods
There is a large range of less conventional construction methods that can all be cate­
gorised under digital concrete. The common thread between these methods is that they
describe construction processes without requiring traditional formwork [5].
1
==PAGE:12==
A major area in digital concrete is additive manufacturing (AM), where structures are
‘printed’ using subsequent layers of material. Other DFC methods include ways of casting
concrete in non­conventional formwork [5, 11].
1.2.1.1
Additive manufacturing
Additive manufacturing with concrete is a process similar to polymer AM and currently of
great interest to the industry. There are two major classes of additive manufacturing that
differ in the way layers of material are deposited: extrusion and particle bed printing.
Extrusion is a method of additive manufacturing where layers of materials are deposited
(extruded) in precise paths to print a volume (fig. 1.1a). This allows for precise manufac­
turing of complex shapes without the requirement of conventional moulds. This method
is very common in 3D printing of polymers and therefore also called 3D concrete printing
(3DCP). [5, 10–12]
Related to extrusion, 3DCP can be used to print formworks (formwork printing) that can be
filled with conventional concrete mixtures (fig. 1.1b). This reduces the volume of special
concrete mixtures specifically designed for layered manufacturing and allows for conven­
tional reinforcement methods in the cast concrete. However, due to the lack of internal
structure and specific loads from the concrete fill, this method comes with common sta­
bility concerns of the printed formwork [11].
Contrary to these extrusion based methods, particle bed printing or powder bed fusion
does not deposit layers of complete concrete mixtures, but deposits a binding material
on subsequent layers of particles or aggregates (fig. 1.1c). With this, previous layers of
particles support the following layers, thus limiting the requirement of temporary support
structures sometimes needed in extrusion based methods. The excess particles can then
be removed after the print, leaving only the volume where binding material has been
deposited. [5]
(a) Extrusion [13]
(b) Formwork printing [14]
(c) Particle bed printing [15]
Figure 1.1: Digital concrete manufacturing methods
1.2.1.2
Other DFC methods
Digital fabrication methods that use non­conventional formwork methods include tempo­
rary supports, slipforming and hybrid techniques [5, 8].
Similar to formwork printing, with the temporary supports method casting moulds are cre­
ated with freeformable removable formwork, such as knitted textiles, which are then used
to manufacture desired volumes (fig. 1.2a). Slipforming uses a vertically moving form­
work, potentially deformable to produce columnar elements (fig. 1.2b). Hybrid techniques
combine DFC methods with conventional manufacturing processes, e.g. to include con­
ventional reinforcement into 3D printed concrete volumes [5, 8].
2
==PAGE:13==
(a) Temporary formwork [16]
(b) Slipforming [17]
Figure 1.2: Other digital concrete manufacturing methods
1.2.2
Digital concrete challenges
Nonetheless, several challenges still remain to be overcome in digitally fabricated con­
crete. The constraints that still limit the applicability of DFC are categorised by Menna
et al. [8] into fabrication, material, construction and design constraints.
1.2.2.1
Fabrication constraints
Limitations in the fabrication process influence the type of structure that can effectively
be manufactured. For extrusion based AM, one of these is the production of overhanging
parts to a structure. Slight overhangs can be printed using corbeling or cantilevering,
where subsequent layers are printed with a slight offset. Larger overhangs require the
addition of temporary support structures, which can be manufactured during the printing
process [11, 18, 19]. These approaches are illustrated in fig. 1.3b.
The requirement for these support structures lead to several significant disadvantages,
including a longer build time and more material usage. The support structures need to
be removed post­print, which can be labour intensive and reduce surface quality of the
structure. Besides that, there is still a staircase effect (see fig. 1.3b), which also negatively
influences the surface quality [18, 19].
For particle bed printing, the main challenge is volume limitations due to a constrained
printing area, as well as the large amount of excess material required.
1.2.2.2
Material constraints
The specific manufacturing processes in DFC require special process­dependent physical
and mechanical properties of the materials used.
There is a significant lack of control over the material rheology of conventional concrete
mixtures, making them less suitable for 3DCP [10]. More workable properties are com­
monly achieved by increasing the cement content in mixtures designed for 3DCP or by
using large amounts of additives [5, 12]. However, this limits the potential environmental
gains achievable with these manufacturing methods.
Other material related challenges for 3DCP are the deformation of placed material layers,
cold joints at layer interfaces and the lack of possibilities for including reinforcement in 3D
printed structures. All these challenges hamper the robustness of concrete AM, which
impairs commercial viability [5, 11].
1.2.2.3
Construction constraints
Transportation and assembly of manufactured elements must also not be complicated,
as gains in labour efficiency can otherwise be lost. As mentioned above, the removal of
additional support material on manufactured structures can be an intensive process.
3
==PAGE:14==
Furthermore, 3D printed structures are currently often manufactured in elements, which
are subsequently combined on­site, commonly with post­tensioned reinforcement [20].
This method limits the type of structures that can be manufactured.
1.2.2.4
Design constraints
The design of digitally manufactured structures is furthermore limited by the used de­
sign methods and their suitability for the manufacturing method. Additionally, digitally
manufactured structures must comply to the relevant guidelines, also limiting the design
procedures. Code compliance is currently often proven with extensive testing [8, 20].
However, for the application of digital manufacturing methods on a larger scale, more
efficient compliance checks should be found.
1.3
Additive Manufacturing with Biopolymer Concrete
Many of the major challenges described above can potentially be mitigated with the ex­
pected characteristics of a further developed biopolymer concrete mixture. Mainly, ma­
terial rheology can be more easily be controlled during 3DCP processes due to ther­
mosetting or thermoplastic behaviour, while potentially disposing of the need for cement.
Additionally, improved tensile and shear strengths over conventional concrete can allow
for improved strategies for printing overhang structures.
1.3.1
Multi­axis Additive Manufacturing
A promising method for achieving non­supporting material printing (NSMP) is the use of
a robotic arm for concrete extruding AM. Whereas conventional 3D printers make use of
3 degrees of freedom (DOF) to print uniform horizontal layers, multi­axis 3D printing uses
additional degrees of freedom provided by a robotic arm or a rotating print bed to print
non­parallel layers with non­uniform thicknesses, or even non­planar layers.
1.3.1.1
Advantages
With multi­axis AM, printing layers can be defined to follow geometrical features of the
model. The tangential continuity method can be used to create layers with a maximised
surface area of contact between each other, which will have a structurally stabilising effect
on an overhanging structure, potentially allowing for printing without additional support
materials (fig. 1.3c) [11, 21, 22].
With NSMP, reductions in manufacturing time and material use can be achieved, as the
normally discarded support structures do not need to be printed and removed. In addition
to this, the method can result in an increased surface quality, as the staircase effect is
reduced and removal operations of support structures are prevented.
(a)
(b)
(c)
Figure 1.3: Multi­axis AM (adapted from [23])
1.3.1.2
Methods
Conventional 3D printing of polymer printing and several concrete printing methods make
use of 3 degrees of freedom in a gantry system, i.e. only translations in space, without
rotation of the model or extruder (fig. 1.4a). To allow for multi­axis AM, rotational degrees
4
==PAGE:15==
of freedom should be added. This can be done by adding rotational operators to the print
bed, while still using a gantry for translational movements (3+2 DOF, fig. 1.4b), by placing
the print bed on a robotic arm with a fixed extruder, thus performing all movements with the
print bed (fig. 1.4c), or by mounting the extruder on a robotic arm with a stationary model,
thus performing all movements with the extruder (fig. 1.4d). Additionally, extra freedom
of movement can be obtained by e.g. by using a supplementary axis for rotation of the
extruder nozzle or by placing the robotic arm on a moving platform or gantry (fig. 1.4e).
(a) Gantry printer [24]
(b) Rotating print bed [2]
(c) Print bed robot [25]
(d) Robot printer [26]
(e) Robot printer on rail [26]
Figure 1.4: Multi­axis extrusion methods
The method with a rotating print bed seems best suited for achieving NSMP, as new layers
can be printed horizontally with previously printed parts straight underneath it. This way,
gravity can be utilised for extruding material, and no large shear forces are immediately
present in newly printed layers. However, for concrete printing of large structures, this
method is more demanding of the printing setup as the mass of the printed structure is
much greater. Therefore, a method with a fixed print bed is preferable.
1.3.1.3
Examples
Examples of 3D printing methods making use of additional degrees of freedom can be
found for both polymer and concrete printing.
For polymer printing, several 5/6­axis printers are commercially available, most based on
5­axis milling machines [27]. Some of these make use of a rotating print bed (Ethereal
Halo [28], Verashape Vshaper [29]), while other ones include the additional axis on a
gantry printer (5axismaker [30]) and even others use robotic arms (TU Delft [25], FreeD
Printing [31, 32]).
In concrete 3D printing, it is common to use robotic arms for printing, but only few seem
to make use of the additional degrees of freedom this provides. Those that do, include
Bruil (fig. 1.5b) [33, 34] and Twente AM (fig. 1.5a) [35, 36]. Other 3DCP manufacturers
that use robotic arms, but seem to print only in horizontal planes are e.g. CyBe (fig. 1.5c)
[37], XreeE (fig. 1.5d) [38], BAM/Weber/Beamix (fig. 1.5e) [39] and Besix (fig. 1.5f) [40].
5
==PAGE:16==
(a) Twente AM [41]
(b) Bruil [26]
(c) CyBe [37]
(d) XtreeE [42]
(e) Bam/Weber/Beamix [39]
(f) Besix [40]
Figure 1.5: Robotic concrete AM
1.3.2
AM with BPC challenges
While additive manufacturing with the Biopolymer concrete seems promising, several hur­
dles need to be overcome before it can be regarded as a feasible construction method on
a large scale.
First of all, further development of the material is still required, and material properties for
and during 3D printing need to be researched.
Secondly, the 3D concrete printer needs development, which includes the design of a
suitable material feeding system and extruder which are suited to the properties of the
material, such as the thermoplastic characteristics and rheology control.
Thirdly, a control application is required that directs the movement of the robot arm. This
application should include a tool for creating a print path which also utilises the material
characteristics. In conventional polymer 3D printing, such a print path is created with the
use of a slicing application, which finds the intersections of uniformly spaced horizontal
slicing planes with a 3D model. For polymer printing, these tools are widely available, as
will be discussed in section 3.3.
However, for concrete 3D printing, these tools are not widely available, which means that
print paths are commonly designed with custom slicing tools, adapted polymer slicing
tools, or by manual definition (i.e. not automatically created from a model). This sig­
nificantly limits any potential of reducing construction workflow [10] and speedy testing.
Furthermore, slicing tools that fully utilise the additional degrees of freedom of a robotic
arm 3D printer for structural applications have not been found, for neither polymer nor
concrete printing.
Still, several approaches to multi­axis slicing have been found in the literature, including
methods with multiple slicing plane orientations [18, 19, 43–45], continuously changing
slicing planes [2, 23, 46] and non­planar slicing methods [25, 47] (see section 3.2). These
methods often use the model geometry for defining the orientation of slicing planes, thus
result in different approaches for different types of geometries.
6
==PAGE:17==
1.4
Thesis aims
Given the potential of additive manufacturing with biopolymer concrete as described above,
this thesis will focus on the development of a slicing and control application that can be
used to automatically create controlled movement of a robotic arm for the use in multi­axis
additive manufacturing, based on 3D objects representing relevant structures in concrete
AM.
This aim will be achieved by firstly reviewing the available literature on additive manufac­
turing, with the focus on multi­axis slicing and printing approaches. Secondly, a tool will
be made to generate a print path from relevant 3D objects. Thirdly, a tool will be created
to get from the designed print path to a controlled movement of the robotic arm, suitable
for 3D printing. Finally, the tools will be used to showcase the potential of multi­axis slicing
methods in structural applications.
1.5
Document structure
Parts of the results from the literature review on additive manufacturing have been dis­
cussed in the current chapter. The next chapter (chapter 2) will contain a discussion of
shapes that are relevant to (multi­axis) 3DCP, which will guide the development of the
slicing tool in chapter 3. Following that, a the relevant numerical control languages are
discussed and the designed print path will be translated to a suitable numerical control
file for the use in a KUKA robotic arm (chapter 4). The application of these tools will be
displayed in chapter 5. Finally, the results and suggestions for future developments will
be discussed and concluded in chapters 6 and 7.
7
==PAGE:18==
8
==PAGE:19==
2
Shapes
As opposed to conventional (3DOF) slicing algorithms, multi­axis strategies make use of
a model’s geometry to determine suitable slicing planes for a 3D object. These strate­
gies are different for different types of structures however, so a general idea of the types
of structures to be 3D printed is required for determining the most suitable slicing ap­
proach. For example, an important difference between structures is the presence of sharp
or smooth edges between structural elements. Sharp edges might be more appropriately
sliced in groups of parallel planes (discrete rotational, see section 3.2.2.1), whereas more
organically shaped structures might be better sliced with continuously changing planes
(continuous rotational, section 3.2.2.2). Furthermore, many multi­axis slicing approaches
consist of some degree of partitioning of the model, which can also be based on geomet­
rical features of the 3D model. Additionally, a clear selection of expected models will help
in testing, visualising and discussing a developed slicing application.
This selection of models will be based on examples of topology optimisation and bio­
mimicry in the literature. These design ideas have been chosen as opposed to con­
ventional structures, as they are optimised with different design constraints and are not
easily manufactured with conventional methods. However, it should also be noted that
the shapes of topology optimised structures, as well as biological structures are also de­
termined with limitations and trade­offs among functionalities [48]. Examples of these
constraints and limitations are material availability, heat and resource transfer properties,
mechanical and structural strength, aesthetics and ’buildability’.
2.1
Biomimicry
Many of contemporary structural ideas can be derived to nature’s structures directly or
indirectly inspiring engineering and architecture for many centuries [49]. The way and
degree in which ideas from nature have been incorporated into engineered structures
differs, and can be classified into four categories according to Ripley and Bhushan [49]:
Biomimetics is the direct copying of biological structures, without adding a layer of engi­
neering or architecture on top. Examples of this are directly copying honeycombs.
Bioinspired structures add this layer of engineering. These structures are inspired by bi­
ology, but are then interpreted, so that only the relevant structural ideas are implemented.
Bioarchitecture adds a layer of art or architecture over bioinspired structures. The layer
between bioinspiration and bioarchitecture is a fine one, as many structures in engineering
also include some degree of aesthetic design or architecture.
Bionic structures is the final category, which involves structures that are not directly re­
latable to biological structures, but are for example designed using topology optimisation
algorithms. These algorithms often involve a degree of simulation and evolution of struc­
tural design, leading to organically shaped structures. This can be argued as an indirect
link to nature as they mimic natural evolutionary strategies [48].
Biomimicry in architectural design has been extensively discussed in the literature [49–
52], even with focus on additive manufacturing [48, 53]. Based on these articles, a cus­
tom selection of biological structures and examples of biomimicked structures has been
made. These models have been categorised again based on their origin, estimated slic­
ing approach and expected printability into the following categories: branching (tree­like)
9
==PAGE:20==
structure, bone structures, shell (dome) structures and cellular structures. Using the pre­
viously described categories, figs. 2.1 to 2.4 have been created.
2.1.1
Branching structures
Branching structures or tree­like structures are signified by the splitting of structural ele­
ments into branches. Figure 2.1 shows a range of branching structures. Tree­like struc­
tures are often output of topology optimisation algorithms (fig. 2.1A) (e.g. [54, 55]), but
are also directly mimicked or inspired (fig. 2.1B).
It can be argued that the use of columns and arches also falls inside this category, as the
rise of Gothic architecture can be led back to the construction of Durham Cathedral in the
UK (fig. 2.1C). For the design of this structure, trees were studied to better understand
the relationship between trunk and branches. This led to improved use of columns and
arches, which allowed for thinner and higher walls, and subsequently to the increased
use of stained glass windows. [49]
This relation is accentuated in the design of the Sagrada Familia (Gaudi), where the
columns are more directly inspired by trees and allow for even bigger dematerialisation
of both columns and walls (fig. 2.1D) [49].
Many of the structures in fig. 2.1 can be constructed with conventional manufacturing
methods. It is expected that (continuous) multi­axis concrete printing will be especially
applicable to these types of structures, as it will be possible to better follow the organic
direction of the structure. This might lead to better interfaces between layers, improved
overhang capabilities and a reduction of support material required [21].

10
==PAGE:21==
2.1.2
Bone structures
In aeronautical engineering, much inspiration has been taken from birds. Specifically the
internal structure of bird bones has been used to optimise the internal design of aeroplane
wings, as they are very hollow, strengthened by either struts or ridges (fig. 2.2A) [60].
The internal structure of bones is locally optimised for the forces it needs to resist. A
clear difference in structure is found between greatly stressed parts of a bone and lesser
stressed parts. This knowledge has been used to optimise structures, such as the Eiffel
Tower (fig. 2.2B) [49].
Other examples are the use of ribs in load carrying structures (fig. 2.2C) and the use
of force diagrams to design trusses (fig. 2.2D). Truss systems can also be the result of
topology optimisation algorithms (fig. 2.2E). These are classified as bionic, as discussed
before.
The internal struts in bones also look similar to organic shapes used in art and architecture
(fig. 2.2F). Examples of these shapes are therefore included as bionically related too.
These types of structures have some overlap with the branching structures discussed
earlier.
This range of biologically inspired structures similar to bone­internals is shown in fig. 2.2,
marked with the letters above. The gap in biomimetic structures is due to a lack of directly
copied structures, as most bone­inspired structures regularise the more random ordering
of internal struts.
Additive manufacturing can improve the design of structures similar to bone internals, as it
will allow for more freedom in the shape of internal struts. The similarity to the branching
structures discussed before will likely mean that both types of structures can be sliced
with similar approaches. The interface between bone­like internal and external structures
might be a more difficult challenge that might need to be solved with distinct strategies.

==PAGE:22==
2.1.3
Shell structures
Shell structures such as domes are not regularly mimicked from nature. Instead, a high
degree of interpretation is applied to reduce the structures to more easily buildable ones.
A range of dome­structures is shown in fig. 2.3.
In engineering, domes are often constructed as a covered skeleton (fig. 2.3A), whereas
nature regularly makes use of cellular or sandwich structures. It is therefore chosen to
classify engineered under bionic, instead of bioinspired. Some architectural projects are
directly inspired by domes from nature, either just as shape or as construction (fig. 2.3B)
[56, 65].
The construction of solid dome walls is something that is expected to be made easier with
concrete AM, especially when using 6 degrees of freedom, as the printing path can follow
the curvature of the dome. 3D printed dome structures are also suggested for use as
extraterrestrial habitats (fig. 2.3C) [66].
The 3D printing of shell structures will mostly focus on solid, thin­walled structures, as the
strutted domes show more similarities to branching structures.

2.1.4
Cellular structures
Cellular structures are seen as structures that have clear, often closed or partly closed
cells. A range of cellular structures, containing both open and closed cells is shown in
fig. 2.4. In nature, these can be both on a macro scale (e.g. honeycombs, fig. 2.4A) or
micro (e.g. bone internals, fig. 2.4B).
This type of structure is in the literature on additive manufacturing discussed as an in­
spiration for infill patterns [61, 73]. This way of using cells is closely related to sandwich
structures (fig. 2.4C).
12
==PAGE:23==
Taking a step further back, cellular structures are sometimes used in architecture, either
clearly inspired by nature or bionically, as buildings are almost always compartmentalised
(fig. 2.4D).
Creating cellular structures using extrusion based AM is challenging, as they consist of
many (unsupported) overhangs. When overhangs or overhang angles are limited, they
become more printable with extrusion based printing [74]. This type of structure in additive
manufacturing is more suitably made with powder bed printing, but raises the challenge
of removing powder in closed cells.
Due to the complexity and many directions of cellular sub­elements, no clear method for
multi­axis AM of these arises.

2.2
Shape catalogue
Based on the biological structures described above, a range of 3D objects is created that
will be used to design and test the developed slicing approach with. The objects are de­
signed in such a way that they range in complexity, allowing for an illustrative classification
on slicability and printability (see section 6.1).
In addition to the models based on the natural structures discussed in the previous section,
a range of basic geometric shapes is included. These models can largely be sliced using a
conventional (3DOF) slicing tool. This type of model can be especially practical for testing
material properties and inclined layer printing (see section 3.2.2.1) in a controlled setting.
2.2.1
Basic shapes
A range of basic geometric shapes is modelled to include most conventional shapes
(fig. 2.5). This includes rectangular shapes such as boxes and walls, as well as a range
of curving structures. All geometries in this category are designed as filled shapes.
13

2.2.2
Branching and arch shapes
The branching structures and bone internals from biomimicry and bionics are simplified
into a range of arches and branching structures (fig. 2.6). The models range in complexity
from simple curved structures to complex ones (i.e. many elements). This will allow for
further evaluation of slicing and printing difficulty.
Even though arches could historically be closely linked to tree­like structures, they are
separated into their own category (fig. 2.7). This is due to the absence of a clear node­
branch hierarchy, which will require an adjusted slicing and partitioning approach.

2.2.3
Shell shapes
A selection of domes and open shells is made for the evaluation of shell like structures
(fig. 2.8). To extend the range of structures to evaluate buildability, models with openings
in the top and sides have been included. A shell part is also included, as well as a shell
with strengthening structures.
All shell models except for the first half sphere are designed as hollow (walled) structures.
Slicing these will result in an inner and outer path, both encircling the empty dome inside.
The final two domes are used to illustrate a more conventional structural approach to
domes, but might be better classified as a combination of branching and arching types. It
is likely that they will require a separate slicing approach from walled dome structures.

A small range of cellular shapes is also included (fig. 2.9). These range in their com­

==PAGE:26==

==PAGE:27==
3
Slicing
Generally in extrusion based additive manufacturing, the path that the extruder follows is
calculated automatically from a 3D model. This is done with the use of slicing software
that calculate the print path from intersections of slicing planes with the model geometry.
For conventional polymer 3D printing, these tools are widely available. These load in a
3D model (most commonly in STL format) and export a file for numerical control of a 3D
printer (most commonly in G­code, see section 4.1).
As discussed above (section 1.3.2), for AM with concrete, these slicing tools are not widely
available. Consequently, custom print paths are directly designed, custom slicing tools
are used or available slicing tools are adjusted. Slicing tools that make use of additional
printing degrees of freedom are even less available. This thesis aims to develop an au­
tomatic slicing tool that is applicable for construction scale 3D concrete printing.
3.1
STL files
Generally in additive manufacturing, the only information required about an object is its
external geometry. This geometry is most often made using CAD software. The de facto
standard for working with this 3D geometry is the STL file. This contains a triangulated
mesh approximating the object’s geometry. Almost any CAD tool can create suitable files,
as no other (more complex) information about the model is required [82].
STL files can be in either binary or ASCII (text) format. They consist of lists of triangular
facets, which are described by a unit normal vector and three vertices or corners. The
normal vector can be used to describe the outer side of the shape, but can also be disre­
garded when the order of the vertices is properly defined (e.g. with the right­hand rule).
However, since the normal vector is not required for describing the geometry, it should
not be relied upon during slicing operations.
An example of an STL file describing a triangular right­angled pyramid is shown below
(listing 3.1, fig. 3.1):
Figure 3.1: Example pyramid STL

Listing 3.1: Example STL file, from [82]
The tessellation of a CAD model into a triangular mesh can lead to a loss of accuracy, as
highly detailed or curved regions are simplified. Likewise, highly accurate STL files will
require many facets, thus increasing file­size and calculation time. Therefore, new slicing
methods are suggested to work around the inaccuracies of STL files, such as direct slicing
and point cloud slicing (see sections 3.2.1.3 and 3.2.1.4).
3.2
Slicing methods
There are two main categories of slicing approaches for additive manufacturing. The most
conventional method creates parallel horizontal planes and is aimed at gantry based print­
ing methods. Conversely, slicing methods with non­parallel layers are in development,
mainly designed for printing methods with robotic arms or rotating print beds (see sec­
tion 1.3.1.2).
Generally, slicing tools consist of a pre­slicing stage (e.g. loading, defining slicing planes),
a slicing stage (calculating the intersections of slicing planes with the model) and a post­
slicing stage (e.g.
hatching, exporting).
The most common and relevant slicing ap­
proaches in the literature will be discussed in the following sections.
3.2.1
Parallel slicing methods
The most conventional slicing tools slice a 3D geometry described by an STL file with
parallel, uniformly spaced horizontal planes. However, other methods are suggested that
work with non­uniform layer heights (adaptive slicing) or that circumvent the need for STL
files (direct or point cloud slicing).
18
==PAGE:29==
3.2.1.1
Most conventional slicing
The most conventional slicing approach is explained in [82] and [83] and not many signif­
icant deviations from this method seem to be widely applied.
The main approach of these methods is to slice a 3D model with uniform, parallel hori­
zontal layers. The intersection of the slicing plane with the model results in a collection of
lines, which are then ordered to create a connected path. These paths can then be used
for further post­slicing operations. The process is described with the flowchart in fig. 3.2.
Figure 3.2: General slicing approach (adapted from [82, 84])
The bound tolerance method as discussed by Choi and Kwok [83] provides tolerant algo­
rithms to execute these steps. The method accounts for numerical errors (e.g. in round­
ing) by allowing a tolerance in the decision on whether model facets should be included in
a slicing operation. Similarly, in the path­generating algorithm (head­to­tail connections),
a tolerance is allowed for, so that small gaps between lines are overcome. This way, even
STL files with faulty facets are sliceable. A number of potential errors still remain, but their
prevalence is significantly reduced [83].
3.2.1.2
Adaptive slicing
A similar slicing approach, but with non­uniform layer thicknesses is adaptive slicing [85,
86]. By increasing the number of layers (decreasing layer thickness) in sections of a
model where greater accuracy is required, the staircase effect can be reduced. Likewise,
sections with more lenient requirements can be printed in thicker layers, potentially reduc­
ing the total print time. This is illustrated in fig. 3.3, where for example the top and bottom
parts of a sphere are printed with thinner layers than the sides or than a (rectangular)
cube.
However, even with thinner layers, the staircase effect will remain to some extent. It has
also been found that the build time is not considerably reduced [83].
19
==PAGE:30==
Figure 3.3: Adaptive slicing [86]
3.2.1.3
Direct slicing
As mentioned in section 3.1, the tessellation of a 3D model into a STL file can result in
losses of model accuracy and greatly increased file sizes. Methods are therefore sug­
gested that slice CAD models directly without the intermediate tessellation step.
However, these methods are not widely used in polymer 3D printing, as they require suit­
able CAD programmes and 3D printers. In concrete 3D printing, this methods seems
more prevalent, with e.g. the use of Rhino, Grasshopper and robot arm integration [87].
3.2.1.4
Point cloud slicing
A different way of bypassing the triangulation step for achieving greater accuracy is by
sampling a model with an optimised point cloud as discussed by [88] (fig. 3.4). By ‘slicing’
the point cloud, a 2D point set is generated. Using the points in adjacent layers, this point
set can be reduced to a simplified set, which is then interpolated to form the print path.
Figure 3.4: Point cloud slicing [88]. Left: model point cloud. Right: 2D point set slice,
simplified set, interpolated print path
This method is found to be more computationally intensive, but the authors argue that it
can be achieved in reasonable time frames with common computing power and that the
computing time is still short compared to the printing time.
20
==PAGE:31==
3.2.2
Non­parallel slicing methods
The aim of multi­axis AM is to achieve non­supporting material printing (NSMP), in order
to increase print quality and reduce material use and build time (see section 1.3.1.1).
Slicing methods for this type of 3D printing are in development and will be discussed in
this section. They are united in that models are not sliced with all parallel layers. Three
main strategies can be identified: discrete rotational, continuous rotational and non­planer
slicing.
Most of these methods are suited to polymer AM with a rotating print bed. Even though
this building method has several advantages with regards to gravity, they are deemed
less viable for construction scale concrete printing. The slicing and printing strategies as
discussed in the literature will therefore need to be adapted to a print setup where the
printed object is stationary and all movements are made with the material extruder.
3.2.2.1
Discrete rotational slicing
The main approach of discrete rotational slicing is partitioning a model into elements which
are then sliced and built along a fixed (non­changing) direction that is suited to the element
[18]. The overall strategy is illustrated in fig. 3.5 and fig. 3.6.
Figure 3.5: Discrete rotational slicing approach (adapted from [18])
The main differences between different discrete rotational slicing approaches is found in
the volume decomposition phase.
The method as suggested by Singh and Dutta [18] partitions a model into buildable and
unbuildable part for a given direction and overhang limit. For each element, a new build
direction is defined. This process is then repeated until all parts have a set print direction.
The boundaries of elements are specified with the use of silhouette curves and build maps.
Smoother transitions can be created by splitting a model in many subvolumes, so that the
slice direction changes more often. An exemplary result is shown in fig. 3.6a.
21
==PAGE:32==
Ding et al. [19] suggest firstly a model simplification step that fills holes in a structure, re­
sulting in a more suitable model for model decomposition. Then a volume decomposition­
regrouping strategy is applied that splits a model based on local curvature and subse­
quently regroups the model elements based on a depth tree. This method yields similar
results to the method from Singh and Dutta [18].
The method as defined by Wu et al. [43] uses the shape diameter function (SDF [89], see
also section 3.5.2.1) to calculate a model skeleton and related shape diameters, which are
subsequently used for partitioning the model following a Gaussian clustering. The model
partitioning approach sets constraints on the intersection of boundary planes in order to
prevent collisions between the print head and previously printed elements. An exemplary
result is shown in fig. 3.6b.
Gao et al. [44] continue on this method, but suggests an optimisation algorithm for defining
the model partitioning planes. This removes the constraints on intersecting partitioning
planes, which reduces the required number of subvolumes while still preventing collisions
with printed parts. An exemplary result is shown in fig. 3.6c.
In contrast to these methods, which all suggest a build direction in the main direction of
subvolumes, Zhao et al. [45] suggest a method for printing overhanging structures with
inclined layers. This allows for printing steeper overhanging structures without rotating
the print bed. An exemplary result is shown in fig. 3.6d.
(a) [18, 84]
(b) [43]
(c) [44]
(d) [45]
Figure 3.6: Discrete rotational slicing methods
3.2.2.2
Continuous rotational slicing
Contrary to the previously described methods, where each model subvolume is sliced
along a fixed direction, the orientation of slicing planes used in continuous rotational slic­
ing methods changes continuously. This results in non­parallel planes with non­uniform
thicknesses, which should allow for the manufacturing of elements with slots and holes
without the requirement of external and internal support structures.
The main strategy for continuous rotational slicing uses a model skeleton to define the
direction of a geometry, to which perpendicular slicing planes are then defined [2]. This
step can be preceded by a partitioning phase, which allows for a more suitable skele­
ton extraction [23, 46]. After slicing, an algorithm is applied to calculate the extrusion
amounts for each line element, as the layer thickness is non­uniform. The overall method
is described in fig. 3.7 and illustrated in fig. 3.8.
Further discussion of skeleton extraction, partitioning and post­slicing calculations follow
in sections 3.5.2.1, 3.5.2.2 and 3.5.4.2.
22
==PAGE:33==
Figure 3.7: Continuous rotational slicing approach (adapted from [2])
Figure 3.8: Continuous rotational slicing approach (adapted from [23])
3.2.2.3
Non­planar slicing
Both discrete and continuous rotational slicing approaches as discussed above make use
of planes for slicing a 3D object. However, other methods exist which suggest printing of
e.g. non­planar layers or wireframes to manufacture a shape.
A method with non­planar layers is suggested by Dai et al. [25]. This method discretizes
a model into voxels, which are then used to define model surfaces on the basis of growth
fields. From these surfaces, a print path is calculated, which combine to form the complete
model. Constraints to prevent collision with previously printed elements of a model are
included in the definition of the surfaces. This method greatly increases the complexity
of the print process, but yields positive results for non­supporting material printing. An
exemplary sliced object is shown in fig. 3.9a.
For quick visualisation purposes, the method suggested by Wu et al. [47] results in printed
objects that represent a model shape by approximating it with a wireframe structure. This
method can greatly decrease printing time, without losing a way of visualising a 3D ob­
ject. Originally, this method is prone to collisions, but a collision avoiding approach is
suggested. An exemplary print is shown in fig. 3.9b.
23
==PAGE:34==
(a) Non­planar slicing [25]
(b) Wireframe printing [47]
Figure 3.9: Non­planar slicing methods
3.2.3
Comparison of 3DOF and multi­DOF slicing approaches
The generalised slicing approaches as described in sections 3.2.2.1 and 3.2.2.2 are corre­
lated in fig. 3.10 (neglecting non­planar methods). This shows that both methods include
many of the same basic parts, but multi­DOF includes additional phases to assist the
definition of slicing planes, as well as additional post­slicing steps.
Figure 3.10: Comparison of slicing processes
24
==PAGE:35==
3.3
Available slicing tools
For conventional polymer additive manufacturing, many slicing tools are widely available.
These tools are often discussed and compared on 3D printing websites, such as [90–95].
From these analyses, it has been found that Cura, Slic3r, Simplify3D and KISSslicer are
the most generally recommended slicing tools. Also often recommended are Octoprint
and Repetier, but these tools make use of slicing engines from the former ones.
A quick comparison of these tools will be made, with the aim of selecting a tool that can
guide the development of a custom slicing tool. For example, it is expected that a slicing
tool for concrete AM should incorporate many of the main features and options that poly­
mer slicing tools include. The four tools mentioned above will be rated on the following
categories:
COM: Comparison Overall placement in cited ranking lists
PRI: Price License costs for regular users (Simplify3D requires paid licence, KISSlicer
has paid PRO and Premium options.
PRC: Printer compatibility Ease of use with different printers
PLU: Plugins Availability of user or expert plugins
SIM: Simulation Availability and complexity of print path simulation
INF: Printing information Information related to print (time, material use)
CAD: CAD software integration Possibility of integration with CAD software
FIL: Compatible file formats Compatibility with different input file types
SET: Printing settings Number of settings that can be changed by the user
SUP: Support structures Availability and customizability of support structures
INF: Infill patterns Number and complexity of infill patterns
USF: User friendliness Ease of use (e.g. simple/advanced input menus)
The tools have been evaluated on these criteria with a simple multicriteria analysis, clas­
sifying them very good (++), good (+) or mediocre (­) (table 3.1, underlying data presented
in appendix A). From this, it can be seen that Cura scores best, followed by Simplify3D
(mainly due to higher price) and Slic3r (due to user friendliness and missing print infor­
mation). KISSlicer misses more features, making it score lower. Based on this, Cura will
be used in this research as the main example of conventional slicing software.

3.4
Custom tool requirements
Based on the analysis of polymer slicing tools (section 3.3), the requirements for the
custom multi­DOF slicing tool can be composed. This includes a list of main features,
as well as relevant slicing options.
3.4.1
Features
From conventional slicing for polymer 3D printing, the following features seem to be
present in most slicing tools:
• STL file loading
• 3D model visualising
• 3D model adjustments:
– Scaling
– Translation
– Rotation
• Support material generating
• Vertical slicing with given:
– Layer thickness
– Shell thickness
– Infill pattern
– Infill density
• Printing speed
• Print path visualising
• Exporting G­code
All of these functions, except for the generation of support material, are also relevant for
a slicing tool for concrete 3D printing. In addition to this, the following features should be
included for multi­DOF slicing:
• Model partitioning
• Model print sequencing
• Non­parallel slicing
• Printer orientation control
3.4.2
Input parameters
From conventional slicing for polymer 3D printing, the following input parameters seem to
be used in most slicing tools. This will be regarded as a reasonable number of settings,
thus to keep operation simple, the aim of the custom tool is to not include many additional
parameters.
• Input file
• Model adjustment parameters
– Scaling factor
– Translation distances
26
==PAGE:37==
– Rotation angles
• Material properties
– Diameter
– Density
– Properties for printing
• Slicing options
– Layer thickness
– Wall thickness
– Top/bottom thickness
• Infill options
– Pattern
– Density
• Support material
– Generate
– Density
For multi­DOF slicing aimed at concrete printing, some of these settings should be ad­
justed or removed, while a few additional settings should be added.
Firstly, the relevant material properties are different for polymer and concrete printing.
For the custom slicing tool, the behaviour of the material during printing is still largely
unknown. Slicing options should therefore be chosen that are independent of material
properties and directly related to the slicing algorithm, instead of slicing parameters being
indirectly derived from material parameters.
Secondly, options relevant to the new slicing approach should be included, such as parti­
tioning and regrouping options, slicing approach and slicing plane orientation limitations.
Thirdly, options regarding the printing shell (number of wall, top and bottom layers) are
less relevant to the structures being printed with concrete AM, because single layer walls
are often sufficient.
Finally, as the method is aimed to achieve printing without additional support material,
no settings for creating these supporting structures are currently needed. Possibly, the
inclusion of this might be desirable in later stages.
3.5
Custom tool operation
Based on the requirements as described in the previous section, a custom slicing tool has
been developed that aims to automatise the slicing process for objects as described in
chapter 2.
In order to achieve support­material free printing, the main slicing approach is based on
the continuous rotational slicing approach as described by Wang et al. [2]. This method
is deemed most relevant for the types of structures that are expected to be sliced, which
are mostly ‘organic’ shapes with ‘smooth’ edges.
27
==PAGE:38==
The main program structure is described in fig. 3.11, which is based on the flowcharts as
described in section 3.2.3. Infill generating is separated from contour processing, to ease
further developments of this part.
Figure 3.11: Custom slicing set­up
The slicing tool has been developed in Python, with use of the modules and their appli­
cations as listed below:
• Trimesh 3.8.12 [96]: STL mesh operations
• Numpy 1.18.5 [97]: Simple mathematical operations, data structures
• Scipy 1.5.0 [97]: Advanced mathematical operations, e.g. rotation, clustering, spline
fitting
• Networkx 2.4 [98]: Network graph management
• Matplotlib 3.2.2 [99]: Plotting
The corresponding scripts are attached in appendix F. Relevant variables and functions
to the following sections are printed in the margins. Overviews are given in appendix C.
Possible error messages that can be encountered are printed in appendix D. The main
variable structure for model elements, slices and contours is visualised in appendix B.

Loading model
# %% model input
The 3D model
load_STL_trimesh()
(also referred to as surface S) is loaded from the STL­file (see
section 3.1) as described by input_filename
input_filename
using the Trimesh module. The
model is then adjusted using the scale, rotation and move
scale, rotation, move
(translation) param­
eters. The operations are performed in that order. The separate operations are
illustrated in fig. 3.12.
Following the model adjustments, a remesh of the model is possible. For each
time as described by the remesh
remesh
parameter, each edge of the mesh will be split
in two. A remesh will result in slightly improved results for the skeleton fitting
phase, as this calculation is dependent on the number of vertices. However,
a finer mesh will also significantly increase computation time. Remeshing is
visualised in fig. 3.12e.
It should be noted that this remeshing stage is based on the already tessellated
model, so lost features will not be recovered. It also does not take the density of
the existing mesh into account, so coarsely meshed model elements will remain
relatively coarse. It is therefore preferable to adjust the meshing parameters in
the CAD programme used (before generating an STL­file).
3.5.1.3
Model checks
After
check_watertight()
loading the 3D model, two checks will be performed on the model. Firstly, it
is checked if the model is ‘watertight’, meaning that no faulty facets are present
e.g. holes in the surface or redundant facets (fig. 3.13). The model needs to
be watertight for the calculation of the skeleton mesh (see section 3.5.2.1), as
the calculation of the surface curvature requires each vertex to be completely
surrounded by vertices. It is also required for capping a model element after
partitioning (see section 3.5.2.2).
Secondly
check_on_bottom()
, the placement of the model with respect to the floor (z=0) plane is
checked. If the model is not placed exactly on this plane, the distance between

3.5.2
Preslicing
The
model_slicing
main step before slicing the model can take place is the definition of the slic­
ing planes. This definition can be based on manual user input (model_slicing =
'normal'), resulting in uniform, parallel slicing planes normal to slice_normal
slice_normal
.
It can also be based on the model geometry, following [2] (model_slicing =
'spline'). For this, a 1D skeleton curve (spline) is calculated from the model
geometry, which defines the orientation of the slicing planes.
As this 1D skeleton curve can only be defined for simple elements without any
splits or branches, the model needs to be partitioned into simple elements. How­
ever, since this partitioning is in some cases based on the skeleton curves of
neighbouring elements, there is some back­and­forth between the calculation of
the splines and partitioning of the model.
3.5.2.1
Skeleton calculation
A model skeleton is calculated to define a 1D curve (spline) that describes the
curvature of a 3D model. The curvature of this 1D spline can then be used to
calculate the origins (equally spaced points along the curve) and normal vectors
(tangential to the spline) of the slicing planes.
Suggested methods
Several methods are suggested in the literature for calculating a model skeleton
from a 3D model. The main options are using the Shape Diameter Function
(SDF) [89], mesh contraction [100], or Mean Curvature Flow (MCF) [101].
31
==PAGE:42==
The Shape Diameter Function [89] is a method focused at calculating the thick­
ness or shape diameter of a 3D model. This is done by calculating the size of
cones that are attached to the inside of a model surface (fig. 3.15a). The shape
(angles) of the cones can be adjusted to optimise the results. As this method
describes the diameter of model elements, a medial curve can be derived from
these results, which lies at half the diameter from the model surface. The shape
diameter can also be used as a measure for partitioning a model, using a Gaus­
sian mixture model (GMM) to group mesh elements together with similar diam­
eters. A significant advantage of this method is that the model skeleton and
partitioning are consistent with model adjustments, such as the repositioning of
animal models.
The mesh contraction method as described by Au et al. [100] (fig. 3.15b) is seem­
ingly the simplest method for calculating a model skeleton. Model curvature is
calculated by contracting the original mesh based on local curvature, i.e. each
vertex of the surface S is moved in the direction of the local surface normal n by
a distance ∆p proportionate to the local curvature H [101]: ˙S = −Hn, ∆p = Hn
The local curvature is described by cotangent laplacian discretezation. Addi­
tional weight parameters are included to constrain the equation. This method is
described in­depth in below.
The Mean Curvature Flow method is derived from the mesh contraction method,
but includes additional terms to improve the conformity to the original geometry
[101]. In addition to the calculations as suggested in [100], the method includes a
factor that attracts the contracting mesh to the medial axis described by Voronoi
poles of the original mesh. To guarantee a good medial axis, the calculation re­
quires a remeshing of the original mesh into a more uniform mesh. The method
results in improved skeleton curves over simple mesh contraction, but is com­
putationally more demanding. The resulting mesh of this method is compared to
the mesh­contracting method in fig. 3.15c.
(a) SDF [89]
(b) Mesh contraction [100]
(c) Mesh contraction (left) and mean curvature flow (right) [101]
Figure 3.15: Skeleton calculation methods
32
==PAGE:43==
Applied method
# %% model skeleton
For the calculation of the skeleton curves in the custom slicing tool, the (simple)
mesh contracting method described in [100] will be applied, as this is computa­
tionally the least complex. However, it is suggested that the skeleton calculation
is improved in future development of the slicing tool, as it is an essential part of
the slicing approach and the current method is shown to be sub­optimal (e.g. not
always in centre of geometry).
Mesh contraction
First of all
calculate_skeleton()
, the skeleton mesh is calculated following [100].
This is still a 3D surface that is the result of contracting the original model surface.
The vertices of this skeleton mesh can then be used to fit a 1D curve through,
resulting in a skeleton curve or spline.
On

equation LV ′ = 0, where L is the n × n curvature­flow Laplace operator with

Spline fitting
The
fit_spline()
vertices of the resulting contracted skeleton mesh are then
used to fit the skeleton curve to. This is done with a Bezier spline of order 3.
For this, the vertices are ordered vertically. To improve the resulting curve, the
top and bottom points inside a given margin (e.g. 5%) are removed, as they
do not always correspond to a well­contracted mesh. Additionally
find_skeleton_ends()
, points can
be ‘manually’ added to the beginning and end of the curve, to further constrain
the spline and prevent extrapolation errors. For example, the centre of node
elements of a branching structure (see section 3.5.2.2) can be added to connect
the splines of adjacent elements, or the centres of bottom contours can be added
to improve the spline orientation at lower parts. In some cases, this spline fitting
can only occur after model partitioning, because of the vertical sorting of vertices.
The process is illustrated in fig. 3.18. Figure 3.19a shows the fitted splines for a
(partitioned) branching structure.
Figure 3.18: Spline fitting process. Left to right: original mesh, mesh contraction,
skeleton mesh, skeleton points, point adjustments, spline fitting
For the case of domes or shell structures, a contracted mesh would not describe
the curvature of a shell wall. In
split_dome()
order to calculate this, a dome structure is split
into sub­elements, for which a contracted mesh would result in a description of
the wall curvature. To split the model, the top of the dome is calculated by taking
the centre of the top (horizontal) slice of the mesh, from which a user­defined
number of vertical planes is defined at fixed angles. For
create_dome_
splines()
each dome element,
the contracted mesh and splines are calculated. These splines can include an
approximate dome top as fixed end point.
The
average_dome_
splines()
splines of the dome elements are then averaged, only taking those with
a total length within one standard deviation of the average length (to discard
faulty splines). For each spline, the origins and normals of the slicing planes are
calculated. The z­coordinates of the origins and the angles of the normal vectors
to the horizontal are then averaged for each virtual slice, which will be used to
slice the dome structure with (see section 3.5.3.4). Figure 3.19b shows the fitted
splines for a simple dome structure.
The setting of a correct number of dome elements (n_elements
n_elements
) is a compro­
mise between the number of facets and vertices in an element (improves spline
approximation), the size of each element (more complex mesh contraction) and
the number of calculated splines (improved averaging). An initial value of 15­20
is suggested.

==PAGE:46==
(a) Spline branching structure
(b) Splines dome
Figure 3.19: Fitted skeleton curves examples
3.5.2.2
Model partitioning
# %% model
partitioning In order to be able to fit correct skeleton curves or splines to the contracted mesh,
the model needs to be partitioned into simple elements which do not contain any
branches and which are only built upwards. This partitioning step is dependent
on the type of geometry and will differ for different structures. Since domes and
simple structures are sliced with a different approach, no partitioning of these
structures is required.
Suggested methods
For the use in a continuous rotational slicing approach, two methods have been
suggested in the literature.
The first method is based on K­means clustering (fig. 3.20a)[46], coarsely group­
ing mesh facets in groups with roughly equal amounts of facets. These groups
are then used to define fine partitioning planes with which the model is split into
buildable elements. However, this method requires the desired model elements
to have roughly equal shapes and does not suggest a specific approach to ele­
ments that connect multiple branches to a structure.
A second method uses the earlier described shape diameter function [23, 89]
(fig. 3.20b, fig. 3.15a). Model parts with similar shape diameters are grouped to­
gether using a Gaussian mixture model (GMM). This method results in a consis­
tent partitioning, but requires the desired elements to have significantly different
diameters. It is therefore unsuitable for elements with roughly equal dimensions.
36
==PAGE:47==
(a) K­means partitioning [46]
(b) SDF partitioning
Figure 3.20: Partitioning methods
Applied method
As neither method is general enough for the types of structures as described
in chapter 2, a custom partitioning approach has been developed for branch­
ing structures and for arches. The applied partitioning method is selected with
model_type
model_type
. No specific constraints are included to prevent collisions of the print
head with printed elements. Manual checks of the slicing results are therefore
required. Further checks with robot simulation software are also suggested (see
section 4.4).
Branching structures
Branching
model_partitioning_
branching()
structures are characterised by a vertically
growing and splitting elements. It is desirable to split these structures into bran­
ches and nodes, such that branches can be sliced using the skeleton curve, and
nodes can be sliced with a specific approach optimised for nodes. It is there­
fore preferable to have a partitioning method that results in as large as possible
branch elements and as small as possible nodal elements. To achieve this, a
variant on the K­means clustering from [46] is designed, consisting of a coarse
and fine clustering step.
The
kmeans_partitioning()
coarse partitioning step searches for the largest number of clusters (K) that
satisfies the user­defined topology. The user defines the number of branches in
a structure (with n_elements
n_elements
), which is compared to the number of branches in
a clustered mesh. The number of branches in a clustered mesh is calculated by
finding the connections between clusters, finding the number of node clusters
and then adding the number of adjacent elements. The number of clusters is
increased until the topology constraint is not satisfied. Figure 3.21a shows the
resulting clustering. Figure 3.21b shows a partitioning with too many clusters,
thus not satisfying the connectivity constraints.
As it is possible that the constraint will be satisfied with an even larger number of
clusters, a parameter is included to continue after not satisfying the constraint.
It will continue searching for a limited number of times and will return the largest
K that satisfied the constraint.
37
==PAGE:48==
Based
combine_network_
edges()
on the connectivity between clusters, non­nodal clusters are subsequently
combined, such that each node or branch element is contained in only one clus­
ter. This is shown in fig. 3.21c. Based
define_boundary_
planes_lstsq()
on this clustering, coarse boundary planes
are defined, which are fitted to the facets linking two clusters. As these facets
do not necessarily lie in well­oriented planes, a subsequent fine partitioning is
applied to improve the plane orientation.
The
create_network()
connectivity between elements is stored in a network graph (fig. 3.21, right),
which is used for further operations.
(a) K­means partitioning max number of K
(b) K­means partitioning higher number K
(c) K­means partitioning combined
Figure 3.21: Adapted K­means partitioning
For
redefine_boundary_
planes_skel()
the fine partitioning, the skeleton curves are calculated for each branch ele­
ment (section 3.5.2.1). These splines can then be used to orient the partitioning
planes normal to the geometry (fig. 3.22, left). These fine partitioning planes
are then used to split the model
cut_model()
. This splitting phase includes a capping of the
model elements, so that each element remains closed. This is required for per­
forming all the post­slicing steps. After this, the caps can be removed (see sec­
tion 3.5.4.4). If a boundary plane cuts the model in several places, resulting in
multiple sub­elements, the element closest to the original (coarse) partitioning
38
==PAGE:49==
and/or with the largest number of facets is retained.
Each
classify_el()
element after partitioning is classified as a node or a branch, which will be
used for further controlling the slicing operations.
Since the original K­means clustering starts with random seeds, the model par­
titioning is slightly randomised. This could result in slightly different clusterings
between slicing operations. For better consistency and for testing purposes, it
might be preferable to find a less random clustering operation. On the other
hand, the randomness also allows for reclustering if the original clustering is not
satisfactory.
The
node_partitioning_
branching()
nodes of branching structures can be further analysed to improve structural
integrity of the printed element. Currently, the model can be repartitioned so that
it can be combined with model branches, or it can be sliced separately along
a normal vector (either with or without boundary caps). The option is set with
node_slicing
node_slicing
. The repartitioning is based on the method as described in [102],
where partitioning planes are defined based on the splines of adjacent elements.
Equidistant planes are defined between these splines, thus splitting the node into
parts which can be recombined with the branches. This partitioning method is
illustrated in fig. 3.22.
Figure 3.22: Branching and node partitioning
Due to the eccentricity of branches in these types of structures, large tensile
forces are expected in the (top of the) nodes, as illustrated in fig. 3.23a. This
would require more advanced approaches to constructing nodes. This could
include a reinforcement technique or improved structural optimisation. It is sug­
gested that further approaches are designed for the 3D printing of nodal ele­
ments, resulting in structural improvements, allowing for a better force transfer
between elements (see e.g. [103, 104], fig. 3.23b).
(a) Sketch of nodal forces
(b) Optimised node design [104]
Figure 3.23: Structural nodes
39
==PAGE:50==
Arches
The partitioning of arches requires a different approach to branching
structures, as arches do not necessarily contain the nodal elements. Instead,
the partitioning of arches is based on a coarse partitioning to split a structure into
single arches, followed by a partitioning to split arches into columns, followed by
a fine partitioning to extract keystones (to prevent collisions). The process is
illustrated in fig. 3.24.
The
find_arch_columns()
initial partitioning into single arches is based on the location of columns on
the floor plane. These are calculated by slicing the model with a horizontal plane
at a low height, splitting the slice into contours and taking the average of each
contour. Then, at each column with two adjacent columns, a partitioning plane is
defined equidistant to the adjacent columns. With these planes, multiple arches
are split into single arch elements.
For
split_arch_coarse()
each arch element, the top of the arch opening is found by slicing the arch
from the top with horizontal planes until the slice results in two contours. The
opening point is then defined as the point in the middle between the two nearest
points of the two slices. At this point, the arch is split into two columns for which
a skeleton curve can be calculated.
The
arches_fine_
partitioning()
keystone is subsequently defined as the element surrounded by the planes
normal to the two column skeleton curves, passing through the opening point.
These planes can also be limited to the slicing angle, in which case the keystone
is defined by planes through the opening point with the limited angle.
Only the keystone partitioning planes are used for the final partitioning of the
complete model, resulting in end columns, centre columns and keystones. The
model_partitioning_
branching()
end columns can be sliced along their splines, keystones are sliced with parallel
planes and centre columns are partitioned again using the branching structures
partitioning method (see section 3.5.2.2). The
combine_arch_
branch_network()
subdivided elements are then
stored along with their (combined) connectivity network.
Figure 3.24: Arch partitioning
3.5.3
Slicing
# %% slicing
After partitioning a model into parts for which the skeleton curves can be cal­
culated, the corresponding slicing planes can be defined for each element of a
structure. There are three options for slicing approaches of elements: mono­
directional (normal), multi­directional (spline) and dome slicing. As discussed
above, most curving sections of a structure can be sliced according to a spline,
40
==PAGE:51==
whereas connecting elements such as nodes and keystones, as well as sim­
ple structures are mostly sliced with parallel planes defined by a normal vector.
Domes are sliced with non­planar slices, which will also be discussed in the fol­
lowing sections.
Each of these approaches use the same slicing function, which is used to extract
the intersection lines of a plane with a mesh. This tool is described first. After
remove_empty_slices()
all
slicing, any empty layers are removed from the element.
3.5.3.1
Slicing function
slicer()
The slicing function used for all slicing operations calculates the intersection of
a mesh with a given plane. The input plane is defined by a point on the plane or
the plane origin P = (x0, y0, z0) and a normal vector to the plane N = [A, B, C].
This definition is translated to a plane equation in the form Ax+By+Cz+D = 0,
corresponding to A(x −x0) + B(y −y0) + C(z −z0) = 0.
To find the faces in the mesh that intersect with the plane, the equation is tried
with each vertex of each face of the mesh to calculate the distance to the plane
(fig. 3.25, 1), using di = N · Vi + D, where Vi is a vertex on the face. If any
of the products of two distances is negative, this means that the two vertices
are on opposite sides of the plane, and the plane thus intersects the facet. The
coordinate of the intersection of the line between these two vertices is then stored
(fig. 3.25, 2). If two such intersection points are found, a line element between
the two points is created (fig. 3.25, 3).
Figure 3.25: Slicer function
This process could be optimised in such a way that not each facet is checked,
but an initial check is made to limit the number of vertices checked. This might
be necessary to improve computation speed for more complex meshes.
3.5.3.2
Mono­directional slicer
The
mono_slicer()
mono­directional slicing tool applies the slicer function to a mesh with planes
as defined by a normal vector and a layer thickness as defined by the user
(slice_normal, layer_thickness
slice_normal,
layer_thickness
). The origins of the slicing planes are cal­
culated by performing a rotation to the original model and its bounding box. The
bottom of the rotated bounding box is used to define the origin of the first slicing
plane and consecutive planes are defined by points at multiples of the layer thick­
ness above this point. All the origin points are subsequently rotated back to the
41
==PAGE:52==
model’s original orientation and the model is sliced. This process is illustrated in
fig. 3.26.
Figure 3.26: Mono­directional slicing planes
3.5.3.3
Multi­directional slicer
The
multi_slicer()
multi­directional slicing tool applies the slicer function to a mesh with planes
as defined using the element’s spline. This
evaluate_spline()
spline is extended with a certain
percentage (e.g. 25%) on either side. The length of the spline is calculated as
S(u) =
∫u
0
√
x′(u)2 + y′(u)2 + z′(u)2du
and the maximum number of slices as
maxNums = 1

where t is the layer thickness [2]
