42
==PAGE:53==
3.5.3.4
Dome slicer
The
dome_slicer()
slicing tool for domes is slightly more complex, as the desired result consists
of non­planar layers that follow the curvature of the dome on any side of the
dome. This is done by taking the average spline (as described in section 3.5.2.1)
and, for each calculated height, slicing the dome structure twice with horizontal
planes at different heights, taking either the outer or inner contour for those slices.
These are then combined to form a single dome slice as illustrated in fig. 3.28b.
The two heights at which the dome is sliced with horizontal planes are a function
of the slicing height and angle as defined by the average spline, as well as the
local wall thickness. This wall thickness is calculated by drawing rays from the
centre axis of the dome and calculating the average of the differences between
the intersecting points of the rays with the shell (see fig. 3.28c). Given the wall
thickness and the slicing angle, the height adjustments dz is calculated as with
dz = sin β × L, where L = cos β × t/2, where t is the wall thickness and β is
the slicing angle (see fig. 3.28d). This adjustment is subsequently added and
subtracted from the slicing height, to give the heights of the slicing plane for the
outer and inner contour respectively.
At these heights, the mesh is sliced with horizontal planes, after which the con­
tours are extracted and their categories (inner or outer) are determined (see
section 3.5.4.2). Only the relevant contour is extracted and the two contours are
then combined to form the dome slice (fig. 3.28b). The layer identification
CW_CCW()
check_inner_outer()
contour_inwards_dome()
and
contracting steps, which are normally performed in the post­slicing stage, are
done immediately, to account for the correct angles and contour type without
having to redetermine these.
(a) Dome angles definition
(b) Dome slicing
(c) Dome slicing wall thickness
(d) Dome slicing variable definitions
Figure 3.28: Multi­directional slicing
43
==PAGE:54==
3.5.4
Post­slicing
# %% post slicing
After extracting the line elements describing the intersections of the slicing planes
with the model mesh, these line elements can be used to determine the print
path. This consists of a step to reorder and connect the line elements, followed
by path processing steps to improve the print path. Furthermore, information
about the print path is calculated and stored. Afterwards, infill can be generated
on the processed slices.
To
slice_to_xy()
simplify the process, slices are first rotated to a horizontal plane with the nor­
mal vector pointing vertically upwards. This is done in two steps, to correspond
to the angles used by the KUKA robotic arm. First, a rotation of angle α around
the z­axis is applied, such that the normal vector lies in the x­z­plane. Second, a
rotation around the y­axis (β) is applied so that the normal vector points upwards.
This is illustrated in fig. 3.29. To account for any inaccuracies, the z­values of all
line elements are fixed to the average value of the slice. The rotation angles are
stored so that the exact inverse rotation can be performed later.
Figure 3.29: Slice rotation to horizontal
3.5.4.1
Path and contour generating
The result of the slicing stage is a collection of unordered and undirected line
elements. These
sort_slice()
are then connected using a path generating algorithm, similar
to the method in [83]. This algorithm is based on a head­to­tail search (fig. 3.30).
The method starts with the line with the maximum x­coordinate. Then, for each
remaining line, the distances to the line’s head and tail are calculated, the closest
line is oriented and then added to the path. This process is then repeated until
all lines are added to the path.
After
link_lines()
ordering the lines, the distances between lines are calculated. Lines that
are within a certain threshold value, e.g. 0.1 mm (link_lim), are linked together.
Distances above the threshold will result in the following stage in a split between
contours.
44
==PAGE:55==
Figure 3.30: Line elements to print path
3.5.4.2
Contour processing
For each contour, the orientation is determined. As seems to be convention
[105, 106], the outermost contour is oriented counterclockwise and all subse­
quent contours switch between clockwise and counterclockwise. The orientation
of a contour could in theory be set with the face normals as defined in the STL
file (see section 3.1), but this is unreliable as the face normals do not need to
be defined to describe the mesh correctly. To account for this uncertainty, the
contour orientation is set with the ray­tracing method as suggested by Volpato
et al. [105].
With
CW_CCW()
this method, a rays is drawn from any line on the contour, perpendicular
to it. The intersection of the ray with all contours in a slice are then counted.
Based on this number, it can be determined whether a contour should be flipped
in orientation (see fig. 3.31). For redundancy, the ray on the other side is also
drawn and the results are compared. If the two rays give different results, the
process is repeated on another line element.
Figure 3.31: Contour orientation
45
==PAGE:56==
After
check_inner_outer()
orienting all contours in a slice, their orientation is determined using the
direction of the line elements on each corner of a slice (max/min x/y).
Subsequently
simplify_path_
on_angles(),
simplify_path_
on_lengths()
, the contours are simplified in order to reduce the number of lines
in the print path. Lines with very similar angles in the plane (difference below
a threshold) are combined. Similarly, lines with a length below a threshold are
combined with their adjacent lines.
Contours
align_contour_start()
are then oriented to start with the lines with the lowest y­coordinate in
the rotated slice. This reduces movement between contours and layers, as well
as aligns potential printing inaccuracies at contour ends.
The
contour_inwards()
following step is contour contraction (fig. 3.32). This step is used to en­
sure that the outside of printed contours correspond to the dimensions of the
input mesh. This is because the print head will follow the print path as a cen­
tre line, thus extruding on either side of the line.
Setting the contour offset
(contour_offset_dist
contour_offset_dist
) to half the extrusion width will result in the material out­
side matching the mesh outside. Each vertex in a contour is moved inwards by
the given distance, using the angles of the adjacent lines to determine the di­
rection. This
contour_inwards_dome()
calculation is adjusted for dome slices, as the initial layer flattening
step is skipped.
Figure 3.32: Contour contraction
Lastly
calc_lines_dist(),
calc_lines_length(),
calc_lines_angle_xy()
, information of the lines is calculated and stored, including the distance
between lines, lines lengths and angles of the lines in the plane (for orienting
non­circular nozzles).
The
path_to_3d()
reverse of the rotation operation is then applied to rotate the slice back to
3D space.
After this
calc_lines_thickness()
, the thicknesses of the lines are calculated based on the Dynamic Line
Thickness method as suggested in [2]. This method calculates the distance be­
tween the centre of each line element and the slicing plane of the previous layer.
This distance is then used as the thickness of each line element using eqs. (3.3)
and (3.4) (adapted from [2]) (see fig. 3.33).
DLT = |xnxSLC + ynySLC + znzSLC −xnx(ui−1) −yny(ui−1) −znz(ui−1)|
√
x2n + y2n + z2n
(3.3)
DLT = PSLC · NT (ui−1) −PT (ui−1) · NT (ui−1)
|NT (ui−1)|
(3.4)
46
==PAGE:57==
Figure 3.33: Dynamic lines thickness (adapted from [2])
As domes use non­planar slices, this step is skipped and the line thicknesses
are fixed to the original layer thickness.
3.5.4.3
Infill
Since the slicing method only generates the external contours of a model, addi­
tional lines can be added to the print path to fill the cross section. It is a common
method for improving surface quality to print the internal features separately from
the external contours. This infill or hatching can function as stabilising material,
helping to prevent buckling instability of the external walls [107, 108]. Also, it
increases the cross sectional area, increasing strength. The infill can be set to
fully cover the area enclosed by the contour, or only fill a user­defined percentage
[109].
Due to the general lack of control over the material in concrete AM, conven­
tional hatching patterns are not often appropriate [11], as they often consist of
non­continuous and overlapping lines. This section will first introduce common
infill patterns and options in polymer printing, as well as continuous suggested
patterns for concrete 3D printing. This is followed by the implementation of two
basic infill patterns.
Suggested approaches
For both polymer and concrete AM, many different infill patterns are suggested,
depending on the process parameters and structure shape and function.
The strength of different infill patterns is studied in [110, 111]. It is shown for
example that hexagonal patterns shows very good mechanical performance. In
addition to the infill, extra perimeter layers increase stiffness and strength of a
printed element.
In addition to the infill pattern, other options can improve the functionality of the
additional material. For example, the overlap between infill and outer contours
can be adjusted to optimise bonding between the two paths. However, this also
poses the risk of overextrusion [112]. Further optimisation can be done by ap­
plying non­uniform infill, where the density of infill is dependent on the stresses
in the structure [113, 114].
47
==PAGE:58==
Polymer AM
For polymer infill, the pattern that is suggested is dependent on the shape
and purpose of the structure, as well as the desired infill density [115]. As discussed in
section 3.3, Cura will be used as a guiding example of a conventional slicing tool [112].
Similar suggestions on infill patterns are found in [84] and [73].
If not a lot of strength is required low densities with simple patterns are sufficient, as they
require little material and are quick to print. The lines and zigzag patterns in Cura are
very similar (fig. 3.34). They both consist of a lines pattern that switches direction every
layer. In the zigzag pattern, the ends of the lines are connected following the wall shape,
see fig. 3.34b.
For most other models, a slightly higher density is recommended (15­50%) with more
complex infill patterns. These patterns include for example planar triangles and hexagons,
which are relatively quick to print, but offer more strength. The grid, triangles and tri­
hexagon patterns are respectively a repeated square, triangle and triangle­hexagon pat­
tern that are equal on every layer. The lines inside the pattern overlap (fig. 3.35).
More demanding prints that require strength in multiple directions benefit from higher in­
fill densities and 3 dimensional infill patterns. These patterns vary from layer to layer
(fig. 3.37). More complex bio­inspired 3D infill patterns are proposed and tested by Po­
droužek et al. [73]. Included in this are a hexagonal pattern, a gyroid infill (similar to
fig. 3.37e) and Schwarz D and Schwarz P patterns. They conclude that custom 3D infill
patterns may be an interesting alternative to classical infills. Examples of these patterns
are shown in fig. 3.36.
Figure 3.36: Schwarz P, Schwarz D, Gyroid and Hexagonal patterns (medium) [73]
For special use cases, there are also patterns that result in flexural prints for specific use
cases (fig. 3.38). With some slicing tools or custom scripts, it is also possible to vary the
infill density in the plane or over height. This can lead to even more efficient material use
[115].
Concrete AM
In concrete AM, the requirements of infill are different to polymer AM due
to process constraints. Mainly, due to a lack of control over the material during printing,
(more) continuous patterns are desired to reduce the number of start/stop operations.
In some cases, the zigzag pattern (see fig. 3.39a) is sufficient. However, for more complex
contours, e.g. combinations of inner and outer contours, adjustments are required to
keep the pattern continuous. Dwivedi and Kovacevic [116] suggest such an approach
that decomposes a cross section into monotone polygons for which zigzag patterns are
created (fig. 3.39b).
Similarly, spiral­like infill patterns allow for more continuous filling, but are also limited to
the complexity of the area (fig. 3.39c) [84].
48
==PAGE:59==
(a) Cura Lines infill
(b) Cura Zigzag infill
Figure 3.34: Simple Cura infill patterns
(a) Cura Grid infill
(b) Cura Triangles infill
(c) Cura Tri­hexagon infill
Figure 3.35: Standard Cura infill patterns
(a) Cura Cubic infill
(b) Cura Cubic subdivision infill
(c) Cura Octet infill
(d) Cura Quarter Cubic infill
(e) Cura Gyroid infill
Figure 3.37: Functional Cura infill patterns
(a) Cura Concentric infill
(b) Cura Cross infill
(c) Cura Cross3D infill
Figure 3.38: Flexible Cura infill patterns
49
==PAGE:60==
Another approach for achieving continuous infill patterns is the use of Hilbert
curves [109] to fill a cross section (fig. 3.39d). However, these curves require
a large number of direction changes in the print path, leading to inaccuracies in
filling the area.
Examples of infill patterns used in contemporary 3D concrete printing include
a ‘connected bottles’ pattern, as used by TU/e (fig. 3.40a) [20] and W or sinu­
soidal patterns used in thin­walled or hollow structures (fig. 3.40b) [117]. A wall
with a sinusoidal zigzag pattern is estimated to have improved characteristics on
thermal insulation and stability compared to traditional wall designs [21].
These infill patterns are mostly aimed at partial infill. For complete infill of a struc­
ture, it is likely beneficial to work with the technique of contour crafting, where
the structure is filled with a conventional concrete mixture (see section 1.2.1.1).
(a) Zigzag infill
(b) Adjusted zigzag infill
(c) Spiral infill
(d) Hilbert curve in­
fill
Figure 3.39: Continuous infill patterns, adapted from [84]
(a) Connected bottles infill [20]
(b) W­infill [117]
Figure 3.40: Continuous concrete infill

==PAGE:61==
Implemented approach
# %% infill
In the custom slicing tool, only lines, zigzag (connected lines) and W­infill pat­
terns are implemented. These patterns are expected to be sufficient for the ex­
pected experiments in the near future. The tool is set­up in such a way that future
additions to infill patterns should be easily implemented. The used infill pattern
is selected by the user (infill_type)
infill_type
.
The
slice_to_xy()
initial step of all infill generating methods is to rotate the slice (back) to the
horizontal plane, as explained in fig. 3.29. This rotation back­and­forth is done
to separate the post­processing and infill generating stages, in order to simplify
future adjustments to either part of the script.
Lines and zigzag
The
infill_zigzag_
generate()
lines and zigzag patterns are closely related, as the
zigzag pattern is just the lines pattern with connected line ends. First of all, the
contours in the slice are virtually contracted (with infill_offset
infill_offset
), to ensure
control over the overlap between infill and external contours. Then, the infill
generating is performed in two steps. First the lines are created based on the
outer contours, after that, the line elements inside inner contours are removed,
such that the infill lines only remain in the inside of the model. The process is
illustrated in fig. 3.41.
Figure 3.41: Zigzag infill generation
The
infill_zigzag_outer()
generation of the infill lines on the outer contours is done by first generating
parallel lines exceeding the contour dimensions, based on the user defined angle
and distances (infill_angle, infill_dist
infill_angle,
infill_dist
). The lines are generated from the
contour centre as defined by the skeleton curve or slicing plane origin, to ensure
alignment between layers. Every other line is flipped to create the zigzagging
motion. Next, the intersections of the lines with the (contracted) outer contours
are found and the exceeding line elements are removed. Following this, the ends
of the lines are connected (if desired, infill_connect
infill_connect
), following the path of the
(contracted) contour.
After
infill_zigzag_inner()
this, the lines are checked for intersections with all inner contours. All line
elements that cross the inner contours are removed, such that only the line el­
ements between inner and outer contours remain. This method does not re­
connect the newly generated pattern, thus requiring extrusion control to prevent
material extrusion on the inner contours. Future improvements can be made as
suggested in [116] to make the infill pattern more continuous.
51
==PAGE:62==
The custom lines pattern does not change orientation between layers, whereas
the Cura zigzag infill rotates 90 degrees every other layer.
The method also struggles for contours that result in multiple crossings of the infill
lines with a single outer contour. These errors can in some cases be prevented
by changing the angle, but might require further developments in special cases.
Due to the non­planar slices of dome structures, the method does not work for
dome structures. However, due to the topology of these structures, W­infill pat­
terns are significantly more suitable in any case.
W­infill
In contrast to the zigzag pattern, which zigzags between outer con­
tours, the W­infill pattern zigzags alternating between inner and outer contours.
This method is especially suited to circular thin­walled structures, such as domes
or tubes. Future developments are suggested to also apply the infill pattern to
thin walls which are not connected end­to­end, thus alternating between a single
outer contour.
Similar
W_infill()
to the zigzag infill, the first step is contracting the contour so that the
overlap distance between external contours and the infill pattern is controlled
(infill_offset
infill_offset
). Then, the points on both the inner and outer (contracted) con­
tour are generated which will describe the ends of each infill line element. These
points are found by drawing rays from the slice centre, as defined by the slicing
plane origin. The number of rays is set (indirectly) by the user.
From these points, the infill lines are drawn, alternating between a point on the
inner and the outer contour. This results in a collection of V shapes, the number
of which is set by the user (W_infill_n
W_infill_n
). The process is illustrated in fig. 3.42.
The z­coordinate of each line element end is taken from the contour it intersects
with, thus allowing for the use with the non­planar slices from domes.
Figure 3.42: W infill generation
Infill post­processing
Similar
calc_lines_dist(),
calc_lines_length(),
calc_lines_angle_xy(),
calc_lines_thickness()
to the post­slicing of the external contours, the
distance between infill lines, line lengths, angles and (dynamic) thicknesses are
calculated. For the case of W­infill for domes, the line thicknesses are fixed to
the user­specified thickness, due to the non­planar slices.
3.5.4.4
Remove caps
After
remove_cap_slices()
all post­slicing procedures, any undesired caps can be removed from the
slices. These caps were required to work with completely closed contours, sim­
plifying all post­slicing. They are now removed by calculating the distance be­
tween the capping planes and all line elements in model elements that are nodes
or connected to nodes. If these distances are below a certain threshold, the line
52
==PAGE:63==
elements are removed. This allows for continuous openings in the printed struc­
ture.
3.5.5
Exporting
After all slicing and post­slicing operations, the resulting print path can be stored
as G­code. The print path is stored as G­code rather than directly in KRL (see
chapter 4), as this is the most conventional method in polymer slicing tools. It also
allows for easier implementation of any potential other printing methods. Besides
this, visualisation of G­code is easier, as many tools are available online.
Due to the model partitioning in the pre­slicing stage, the elements need to be re­
combined before being exported. Furthermore, the angles of the printer extruder
need to be defined.
3.5.5.1
Print order
The generated slices can be reordered to form the complete print path in sev­
eral ways (see fig. 3.43). The user is asked to specify the desired print order.
Currently this is implemented by a text input.
Firstly, each model element can be printed sequentially (fig. 3.43a). This method
reduces movement of the printer, as subsequent layers are close to each other.
Between model elements, the printer will be stopped and can be manually moved
to the beginning of the following element. This is done to prevent collision with
the previously printed parts.
Secondly, several elements can be printed simultaneously (fig. 3.43b).
The
printer will alternate between the selected elements and print a single slice on
either element. This method reduces the time between printing elements, thus
limiting the risk of cold joints.
Thirdly, slices could be recombined in blocks (fig. 3.43c). This method com­
promises between between the previous two, thus limiting movement and cold
joints. However, this method has not been implemented yet.
For models that are sliced in one part, this step is skipped.
(a) Elements
(b) Combined elements
(c) Blocks
Figure 3.43: Printing order
For each slice, the print order is set to first print the (outer contours) in order of
which they are numbered, followed by the infill patterns. Reordering this might
be necessary in order to improve print and surface quality.
53
==PAGE:64==
3.5.5.2
Printer orientation
The
angles_to_ABC()
orientation of the printer extruder is generally based on the normal vector of
a slicing plane. The angles of the slicing normal are translated to tool angles for
KRL. The tool angle is described by the angles A and B, similar to the rotation
angles as described in fig. 3.29. A defines the rotation around the z­axis, then B
describes the angle to the horizontal plane. The angle C is used to then define
the placement of the robot arm in respect to the extruder tool. The angles are
translated to a value between ­180 and +180 degrees.
For
ABC_dome()
domes, the B angles are based on the slicing orientation defined by the
curvature of the dome wall. The A angle is described by the angle between the
centre axis of the dome and the location of the centre of each line element.
The tool angle B can be limited by the user (print_angle_lim
print_angle_lim
) to prevent col­
lisions of the extruder with printed parts, as well as with the robot arm itself. It
may also allow for improved accessibility of more difficult to reach print paths.
3.5.5.3
G­code generation
Next
path_to_gcode()
, the print path and all additional information is added to the G­code file
(output_filename)
output_filename
. For lines on the designed print path, the G1 motion com­
mand is used and for lines connecting contours or slices, G0 is used. This line
type is based on the distance between two subsequent lines. For further expla­
nations of G­code, see section 4.1.
The G­code for the print path includes the calculated tool orientation angles (sec­
tion 3.5.5.2) and information regarding the extrusion volume. Normally the extru­
sion amount is directly given in a G­code. However, for the use in concrete 3D
printing, the extrusion volume is instead translated into a difference in printing
speed, thus removing the need for precise extrusion control and instead using a
constant flow of material. The line speed is defined as follows:
vline = Qextrusion
tline · wline
(3.5)
where vline is the linear speed of a path element, Qextrusion the extrusion speed
(flow rate), as given by the user (extrusion_speed
extrusion_speed
), tline the thickness of a line
as calculated with the DLT method (eq. (3.4)) and wline the printed path width as
given by the user (line_width
line_width
).
For line elements without an extrusion value, the line speed is set to the user
defined moving speed (move_speed
move_speed
).
Additionally, the slicing parameters are stored as comments at the beginning of
the G­code file, which makes it easier to reproduce the print path.
During
calc_volume()
the generation of the G­code from the print path, the lengths of the line
elements are stored and summed. This allows for the calculation of a total print
path length, and thus material volume. Given the extrusion speed, an approxi­
mate print time is also calculated.
54
==PAGE:65==
3.6
Future developments
Some areas for future developments of the custom slicing tool have already been men­
tioned in the previous sections. An overview of the most important areas will be given
here. Further discussion is done in chapter 6.
3.6.1
Pre­slicing
The most important elements of the slicing tool are the use of the skeleton curve for defin­
ing the slicing plane orientations and the preceding model partitioning. Both elements can
be improved. It has been mentioned that superior skeleton calculation methods exist (see
section 3.5.2.1). These give better results, but are computationally more advanced. It is
suggested that improvements are made in the implementation of the skeleton calculat­
ing algorithms, as it has been found that the current method can yield imprecise curves.
These improvements should focus on the implementation of an efficient remeshing stage,
to better spread the vertices of the model. Furthermore, the inclusion of a medial axis to
attract vertices to, should result in skeleton lines that better follow the geometry of the
model [101].
The current partitioning methods seem sufficient for the types of structures tested. How­
ever, new partitioning strategies might be required for different models (see section 6.1).
For example, partitioning methods for arches with additional superstructures could be im­
plemented to combine arches with walls. Another example would be domes with holes
or connecting structures, where holes could be filled and later removed, and adjacent
structures could be printed simultaneously.
Furthermore, strategies on the partitioning and slicing of nodal elements should be devel­
oped that take the forces in nodes better into account (see section 3.5.2.2).
In some cases, additional support structures might still be desired for extremely over­
hanging model elements. This could also be included, similar to the implementation in
polymer slicing tools.
3.6.2
Slicing
The current slicing stage is quite straightforward. Future developments should look into
improving the efficiency of the current methods, as well as to include new slicing strate­
gies, such as inclined layer printing (section 3.2.2). Also, including automated calculation
of slicing parameters based on material properties could be a desired feature in the future.
The implemented slicing approach does not function well with horizontal surfaces or more
generally surfaces parallel to the slicing plane (fig. 3.44 top). Conventional slicing tools
apply special techniques for designing a print path for these surfaces, including additional
support structures. The current models do not require these techniques, but future models
might require further development of the slicing method to include these surfaces (fig. 3.44
bottom).
Future developments should also focus on the implementation of collision constraints in
the partitioning and slicing approach.
55
==PAGE:66==
Figure 3.44: Horizontal surfaces (top: ignored, bottom: added)
3.6.3
Post­slicing
In polymer 3D printing, it is not unusual to print walls with multiple contours, to improve
stability (fig. 3.45). In concrete AM, this is often less necessary, but in some cases it
might still be desired. Implementation of such strategies should be simple with the current
functions already in the slicing tool (notably contour contracting).
Figure 3.45: Extra walls
As discussed in section 3.5.4.3, many more infill strategies are suggested in the litera­
ture. First of all, the current methods should be improved, particularly to fix problems
with zigzag lines crossing contours multiple times, as well as to implement W­infill for thin
walls with only outer contours. Later developments should look at the implementation of
improved (continuous) infill patterns.
As mentioned in section 3.5.5.3, including a regrouping strategy of slices based on blocks
of slices might be implemented to balance extruder movement and cold joint prevention.
56
==PAGE:67==
4
Numerical control
Most conventional polymer 3D printers use G­code for the control of the printer. In the
current setup, a KUKA robotic arm is used, which uses the KUKA robotic language (KRL)
for its control. Both languages are text and number based, automated control of the
machines and can therefore be classified as Numerical Control (NC) methods.
The similarities between G­code and KRL allow for simple translation of most commands,
as most command types have an almost one­to­one equivalent. Since the NC is almost
exclusively used for instructing motion, it poses no significant problems for the 3DCP
method to first generate G­code and then translate to KRL. The biggest advantage of this
method is that the slicing tool is more easily applied to other printing methods as well.
This chapter will first introduce the structure of both G­code and KRL languages. Next
the translation method is described and illustrated.
4.1
G­code
G­code files are files written in the G­code language containing instructions for computer
aided manufacturing. In additive manufacturing (AM), these files are often created by
slicing software (e.g. Slic3r, Cura, see section 3.3), but can also be custom made using
libraries (mecode) or written manually [118]. The code consists of a list of commands in
the form of a single letter followed by a number, or combinations hereof. An example of
a typical G­code command in additive manufacturing is:
G1 F500 X25.00 Y33.33 E1.80
The letters define a certain command and the numbers generally determine a parameter
value. The most common letters are summarised below (table 4.1):
G
Motion
X
Horizontal position
Y
Vertical position
Z
Depth
F
Feed rate
S
Spindle speed
T
Tool selection
M
Miscellaneous functions
I, J
Incremental centre of an arc
Table 4.1: G­code command types [119]
G­code commands are modal [119], which means that values are set until they are ac­
tively changed. In conventional printing this means that a position command generally
only includes values for X and Y, while Z values are only given when continuing on a dif­
ferent layer. Similarly, printing velocities only need to be given when they are supposed
to change.
In 3D printing, the file mainly consists of a list of linear movements (G1) such as the exam­
ple above. Here G1 commands a controlled linear movement to a point with coordinates
X=25 (mm) and Y=33.33 (mm) and E1.80 sets the extrusion amount to 1.80 mm. Other
57
==PAGE:68==
motion commands include G0 for rapid movement and G2 and G3 for circular motion clock­
wise and counter­clockwise. Some motion parameters can also be set with G­commands,
such as G90 and G91 for absolute or relative programming and G92 to set positions to zero.
Units are set to millimetres with G21 and to inches with G20.
Common other commands include F.. to set the feed rate or velocity (combined with
G­commands) and a range of M­commands setting parameters specific to the printer me­
chanics (e.g. fan speed, temperature, acceleration, wait). Comments start with a semi­
colon (;) and can be made anywhere on a line.
A virtually complete list of possible G­code commands is given and kept by Reprap [118].
4.2
KUKA Robot Languange
The KUKA robot arm available works with KUKA Robot Language (KRL). These files can
be written on the control panel (KCP) connected to the robot controller, or can be made
externally and loaded onto the KCP. A notable difference with G­code is that KRL is more
regularly used to also perform programming tasks (e.g. loops, if­statements), whereas
G­code is generally only used to list sequential commands.
A KRL programme is made up of SRC and DAT files. The SRC­file contains the actual
programme code, while the DAT­file contains specific programme data [120]. A file starts
with DEF or DEFDAT and ends with END or ENDDAT. The file then first contains all declarations
(defining variable types and names), followed by all statements (the actual programme).
The code files are compiled by the KUKA software upon closing a file or when copying
to the device. By selecting a programme file, all relevant files are linked to create a
programme.
Programmes in KRL can contain FOLDs, which hide the specific lines of code inside them.
The commands INI and PTP HOME are common examples of these which are included in
KUKA templates. Generally, a KRL programme starts with both these lines and ends with
PTP HOME.
The KUKA Robot Language allows for much greater complexity in the scripts. The most
relevant commands for 3D printing movements will be summarised here. Further details
can be found in the Expert Programming Manual [120]. Similarly to G­code, most com­
mands in KRL are modal.
In addition to the initialisation commands described above, several other parameters
should be set at the start of the script file. These include the tool and base selection. The
tool and base can be defined using the KCP and are selected with $TOOL = TOOL_DATA[n]
and $BASE = BASE_DATA[n]. Other system parameters also start with $ and can be set
whenever required. Examples of these are the tool orientation during linear movements,
and approximation parameters. System parameters keep their set values until a script file
is deselected.
Motion commands can be given as point­to­point movements (PTP), controlled linear move­
ment (LIN) and controlled circular movement (CIRC). These syntaxes are followed by the
(Cartesian) coordinates of the destination point, including rotational specifications. Cir­
cular motion includes the statement of an auxiliary point on the arc. An example of a
command is
LIN {X 10.0, Y 20.0} or
PTP {X 10.0, Y 20.0, Z 0.0, A 20, B 7.5, C 90}.
58
==PAGE:69==
Control of speed and acceleration can be done by changing the system variables $ACC.CP
and $VEL.CP.
Orientation control of the tool ($ORI_TYPE) makes it possible to fix or allow the orientation
of the tool during controlled motion. The options are illustrated in fig. 4.1 [120].
Figure 4.1: KUKA orientation control [120]
It is also possible to allow approximation of points along a path, which makes smoother
motions possible (the robot arm does not have to slow down to reach exact points). The
allowable approximation can be set with parameters such as $APO.CDIS for LIN­LIN ap­
proximation. Figure 4.2 shows an example of a path with approximations. Further devel­
opments should also include the possibilities for approximations based on velocity and
orientation.
Figure 4.2: KUKA path approximation (adapted from [120])
As discussed in section 3.5.5.3, the print process can paused between elements. This is
done using the HALT command. User input is then required to continue the programmed
path.
4.3
Translation
Most relevant commands in G­code have a direct KRL equivalent. These can be easily
translated with a simple translation tool. The translation tool is developed in Python and
presented in the appendix (appendix G). The translation tool is based on the correlation
table displayed below (table 4.2).
The translation tool will translate the file as put­in by the user (input_file). The transla­
tion tool allows for some manual adjustments by the user. These include a scaling factor
59
==PAGE:70==
and translation distance (offset). Additionally initial values can be given for the robot
speed and acceleration, if these are not given in G­code. Limits to the speed and accel­
eration are also user defined. Other parameters that are specific to KUKA, are also set
by the user. These are the approximation parameters and base, tool and tool orientation
settings. Furthermore, a start position can be given by the user to which the robot arm
will move before any of the G­code commands. An example of the user input parameters
is given in listing 4.1.
4
# %% INPUT
5
6
# files read/saved as txt
7
input_file = 'output/DTU_jul.gcode'
8
output_file_name = 'output/DTU_jul'
9
10
# coordinate scale
11
scale_coor = 15
12
scale_speed = 1e5
13
scale_acc = 1
14
15
max_speed = 3.0 #m/s
16
max_acc = 5.0 #m/s2
17
18
offset = [-1500,1000,0] # x, y, z [mm] appllied after scale
19
20
# movement parameters
21
speed_ini = 3.0 #m/s
22
acc_ini = 5 #m/s2
23
approx_LINLIN = True
24
approx_dis = 10 # mm
25
26
# header and footer of KRL SRC file
27
BASE = '$BASE = BASE_DATA[1]'
28
TOOL = '$TOOL = TOOL_DATA[3]'
29
ORI =
'$ORI_TYPE = #VAR'
30
31
PRINTSTARTPOS = '{X 0, Y 0, Z 200, A -180, B -90, C -90}'
Listing 4.1: Input parameters
The translation tool consists of several functions that each translate a specific command
type. These are described below. The core of the tool then uses these functions to
translate the G­code file line­by­line.
It should be noted that the units in G­code and KRL differ for acceleration and velocity.
These are translated accordingly (see section 4.3.3). The definition of circular motion is
also different between the two languages. Circular motion in G­code requires the relative
position of the arc centre point, whereas KRL requires the (absolute) coordinate of an
auxiliary point on the circle (see section 4.3.2).
Parameter changes, such as speed and acceleration are done with separate lines in KRL,
and thus require special treatment by the translation tool. A G­code command is first
checked for these changes, which are then stored and printed to the KRL file before the
following motion command.
Comments are copied directly from G­code to KRL. Any commands in G­code that are
not included in the translation tool will also be included as comments.

4.3.2

The
translate_motion()
motion type in G­code is given with a G­command. These are separated
from any additional information, such as coordinates, and translated first. This
follows the correlation described in table 4.2. The
translate_coor()
coordinates related to the
motion are then translated and put in the correct format. The scaling factor and
offset (scale_coor, offset
scale_coor, offset
) are applied. Angle definitions (ABC) are included in
the translation of coordinates.

translate_circ()
the motion type is an arc, a special translation is necessary. In G­code an arc
motion is defined as the end point and the relative position of the arc centre, as
well as the direction of the arc (clockwise or counterclockwise) as

where I and J give the distance from the start point to the centre. In KRL, an arc
is defined by the end point and a point on the arc as
CIRC {X .., Y ..},{X .., Y ..},
where the auxiliary point is defined first and the end point second. The points
are illustrated in fig. 4.3. An example is shown in listings 4.10 and 4.11.

4.3.3
Speed and acceleration
In G­code, speed parameters are changed in combination with a motion com­
mand (G.. F..), whereas acceleration changes are set with a specific command
(M204 S..). In KRL, both are done with specific commands. These changes
are extracted before translating motion commands, stored, and included before
printing translated motions to the SRC file.
Speed
translate_speed(),
translate_acc()
and acceleration in G­code are defined in [mm/min] and [mm/s2], whereas
KRL uses [m/s] and [m/s2]. These units are translated correspondingly.
The translated values are scaled and capped with the user­defined values (scale_
speed, scale_acc, max_speed, max_acc)
scale_speed,
scale_acc, max_speed,
max_acc
. The KUKA robot arm used requires
limits of 3 m/s and 10 m/s2 for speed and acceleration respectively [120]. As
noted before, both G­code and KRL are modal, so settings keep their values
until changed.
Listing 4.12 and listing 4.13 display the translation of speed parameters. List­
ing 4.14 and listing 4.15 display the translation of acceleration parameters. The
speed and acceleration are capped at 2.0 m/s and 5.0 m/s2.

4.3.4
Exporting
The translated commands are stored as text in the SRC file with a user­defined
file name (output_file_name
output_file_name
). The corresponding DAT file is also stored, but
does not contain any information from the G­code.
4.3.5
Discussion
The translation tool is designed for translating the G­code files as generated by
the custom slicing tool. Several translation functions are therefore not included.
Firstly, it is assumed that the G­code commands are given in metric units, thus
ignoring any G20 or G21 commands. Secondly, circular motions are only trans­
lated in the x­y plane, further development of this have not been made, as 3D
circular motions are not expected in G­code created by a slicing tool.
Thirdly
translate_extrusion()
, no effective way of giving extrusion commands to the robot arm have
been found. This requires further research into the KUKA robot arm. As the
custom slicing tool uses velocity changes for controlling the amount of material
deposited, the E­commands are not used. If required in later stages, extrusion
values could be linked to the KRL programme by outputting integer values using
binary output channels. These should then subsequently be interpreted by the
extruder.
4.4
Simulation
It is desirable to be able to simulate the created numerical control files, so that
any problems with the files can be identified in early stages. For G­code files,
simulation tools are widely available. In this thesis, the online tool NC­viewer
[121] has been used, but many other tools are at hand. For KRL files, these
tools are not as easily accessible. These tools are generally more complex, as
they include simulation of the movement of the robotic arm. Several paid simula­
tion tools are available, including KUKA Sim [122], RoboDK [123] and RoKiSim
[124]. In combination with other software packages, such as Grasshopper, robot
simulation can be included [87] (see also section 3.2.1.3).
66
==PAGE:77==
5
Showcase
To demonstrate the outcome of the developed slicing and translation tools, the following
chapter will illustrate the prints of several structures. For each of the main categories (sim­
ple, branching, arch, dome), a print path has been calculated, translated and traversed
for a simple structure of that type. The whole process and process parameters will be
displayed for each of these showcases.
Since the concrete printing process is still in development, it has not been possible to
demonstrate the slicing application by printing concrete. Instead, the printing traverse is
visualised using a light on an extruder dummy by taking long exposure photographs and
combining these images. Animated versions of the visualisations are also made available.
The pictures have been taken with varying exposure settings and printing speeds, which
have not been set to represent the concrete printing process. Any conclusions regarding
printing speed or time should therefore not be drawn on these tests.
The prints have been performed in a combination of T2 and automatic mode (see [125]).
The robot was controlled manually in such a way that motion only occurred when the
camera shutter was opened. Due to slight inaccuracies in this method, some gaps might
occur in the visualised print path.
The figures that illustrate the slicing process have been recreated after the original slicing
procedure and might therefore show slight differences to the actual slicing process used
in the visualisations. However, these differences are expected to be very small.
In addition to the ‘printed’ models, for each type of structure, two additional examples are
sliced to illustrate the slicing process further.
For comparison, the same structures have been sliced using Cura (see section 3.3) us­
ing equal layer thicknesses, a similar infill pattern (20% zigzag, 40% triangles or none)
and without generating top/bottom layers. To illustrate the potential savings of the slic­
ing method, Cura is set­up to calculate support material for overhanging parts exceeding
50°. Table 5.1 shows the comparison for all examples, including the calculated volumes.
Note that these supports are designed for polymer structures and might be different from
supports for concrete structures.

6
Discussion
The previous chapters of this thesis have shown the method of getting from a 3D model to
a working print path traversed by the KUKA robot arm. The illustrated method has used a
continuous rotational slicing approach to make used of the additional degrees of freedom
that the robotic arm and the biopolymer concrete allow for.
The implemented method is shown to be applicable to the types of structures as expected
from topology optimisation and biomimicry design methods. However, the method is not
fully complete and optimised. Most importantly, the slicing and partitioning methods used
are highly dependent on the geometry and properties of the input models. Different types
of models than the ones used to test the tool might need additional fine tuning of the
used scripts, a further re­ordering of existing functions or even considerable additions.
Section 6.1 will discuss this further.
Secondly, due to the limited development time frame, many of the applied methods and
functions in the slicing script have not undergone an optimisation stage and can likely
be improved computationally and extended with additional features. The structure of the
scripts has been made to ease these further developments. The areas of the script that
further developments should be directed at are discussed in section 6.2.
Finally, the slicing method has not been tested with the material it is intended for, resulting
in a slicing tool that is based on assumptions and estimates of what the material and
printing method will allow. It has been attempted to make the scripts as generic as possible,
with many attachment points for further developments, but further developments should
be guided by tests applying the slicing method with the material and finding the current
limitations to the developed tool. Suggestions on tests related to parts of the slicing method
are made in section 6.3.
6.1
Model classification
Based on estimations of the BPC material characteristics and the tests with the slicing
tool, estimates have been made on the types of structures discussed in chapter 2. These
structures have been classified based on their expected sliceabilty and buildabilty. The
slicability shows whether a structure can be sliced to a useable print path using the current
set­up of the script by just changing the input parameters (minor fine­tuning), if it can be
sliced with the existing functions, but only after some adjustments and re­ordering, or
whether entirely new functions are required. Buildability is an estimate on the difficulty
of building the structure to be statically stable. This includes buckling concerns, potential
requirements for additional support materials and collision concerns of the extruder with
printed parts. The classification is shown in fig. 6.1.
Many of the structures as shown in chapter 2 or similar to those are sliceable with the
existing scripts. Some more complex structures might need new or adjusted partitioning
steps, such that slicing planes are defined that take the model geometry better into ac­
count. An example is an arch structure with additional superstructures (fig. 6.1A). It would
be desirable to separate the arch and wall (either using the slicing tool or outside of it),
such that the arch can be sliced following its geometry and the wall is sliced with horizontal
planes. Both structures should then be combined to create the print path for the complete
structure. Similarly, structures consisting of many connecting walls (e.g. honeycomb or
83
==PAGE:94==
Only fine-tuning
Minor adjustments
Major adjustments
Sliceable →
Buildable ↓
Okay
Minor stability concerns
Collision concerns
Major stability concerns
A
B
C
C
D
E
E
Figure 6.1: Model classification
84
==PAGE:95==
2D Voronoi models, fig. 6.1B) might require adjustments to the partitioning method to im­
prove clustering of wall elements. More complex structures that consists of combinations
of structure types (e.g. arches and branching or domes and arches) would require even
further developments on model decomposition and recombining methods (fig. 6.1C).
With regards to buildability, much is still unknown. Therefore, there are minor stability
concerns for many structures with overhangs. Tests on the allowable overhang angles
and layer orientations should result in a better categorisation of these types of models.
Structures without any ‘inherent’ supports might require further external support material
(fig. 6.1D). Furthermore, some structures, such as closed domes and connected wall el­
ements, pose a high risk on collisions of the printer with printed parts of the structure
(fig. 6.1E).
6.2
Future slicing developments
Due to the limited time frame of the slicing tool development, the main focus has been set
on creating a tool that goes through every necessary step required for multi­axis slicing
and which allows for easy implementation of further features. This also means that the
tool is not as optimised, general or multifaceted as might be desired. Three main areas
where further developments are possible are the volume decomposition and regrouping
approaches, slicing methods and post­slicing procedures.
6.2.1
Volume decomposition
The pre­slicing stage of the implemented slicing method requires a volume decomposition
stage to allow for calculation of model element splines. Partitioning methods have been
developed for simple branching and arch structures, but additional steps are required for
more complex structures as discussed in section 6.1.
The strategy of inclined layer printing (section 3.2) should be investigated for simple struc­
tures, as well as for more complex structures not described by any of the other methods,
decomposing the model into elements which are printed with a single slice orientation.
For branching structures, developments should focus on testing and adapting the current
method on more complex structures, including those with more branches and those where
branches reconnect. Further investigation in node design should be done to find a strategy
that results in a structurally strong element. Furthermore, strategies for recombining model
parts (as discussed in sections 3.6.1 and 6.1) should be tested and further developed.
These methods are dependent on the risks of cold joints and on the possibility of (safe)
non­printing movements.
For arches, developments should be directed at arch structures with additional superstruc­
tures, such as walls. These two parts should be separated to allow for multi­directional
slicing of the arch element and mono­directional slicing of the superstructure.
For domes and shell structures, improvements should be made with regards to ’incom­
plete’ domes, i.e. domes with openings, as well as with additional (side) structures. This
could include a hole­filling step, such that the current slicing method can be used, fol­
lowed by a step of removing the fillings. Furthermore, a decomposition into a dome part
and side­structures should allow for specialised slicing approaches on either structure.
Furthermore, the current method of partitioning a model and capping sub­elements with
planes that require slight random adjustments could be improved upon. This could for
example be done by using a custom plane cutting method instead of using the existing
Trimesh function.
85
==PAGE:96==
6.2.2
Slicing method
The current tool uses the continuous rotational slicing method as discussed in [2] and
section 3.2.2.2, as this is expected to be the most suitable to the types of structures in
construction­scale 3DCP. However, other slicing methods should be investigated to test
whether this assumption is correct. The most suitable alternatives are expected to be the
non­planar method as discussed in section 3.2.2.3 and inclined layer printing as discussed
in section 3.2.2.1.
Related to the slicing methods, the potentials of both adaptive slicing and direct slicing
should be investigated, potentially improving slicing speed, construction time and build
quality. Also, point cloud based slicing could be of interest to prevent tessellation errors.
Furthermore, developments on the current slicing method should focus on optimising the
current slicer function and skeleton calculations, as discussed in sections 3.6.1 to 3.6.3.
Relating to the print method, collision constraints should be implemented in the slicing tool
to prevent collisions of the extruder or robot arm with printed elements or itself. Limitations
on the range of the robot arm, as well as robot arm simulation steps can also be included
to ease these challenges.
6.2.3
Post­slicing
The current post­slicing stage includes all necessary basic steps for generating a useful
print path from the intersection lines of the slicing plane with the model. However, further
developments are possible, mainly focusing on infill patterns.
The current tool includes two infill methods: zigzag and W­infill. Further infill patterns as
discussed in section 3.5.4.3 could be included. Furthermore, the zigzag infill pattern can
be improved in order to create a more continuous path, as well as to fix problems with
complex shapes (see section 3.6.3).
Additionally, the post­slicing step of rotating a slice to a horizontal plane could be improved
by using local coordinate systems instead of rotating the plane in the global system. Also,
a dynamic layer thickness approach for non­planar dome slices should be developed.
The translation tool from G­code to a KRL path does not require many improvements.
Potential additions of new G­code commands might be necessary if they come up. Also,
further approximation control might be desired.
6.3
Material tests
Further development of the current slicing tool should be guided by printing tests with the
robot arm and the BPC material. Tests regarding the slicing tool should focus on testing
the accuracy of the designed print path and on finding the relation between the material
properties and relevant slicing parameters. It is expected that only the simple structures
will be used to test the material in the initial phase.
First of all, the calculated print path should be tested with the material and robot arm to
investigate the accuracy of the followed print path, the ‘smoothness’ of the path, especially
regarding velocity control, and the accuracy of extruded volumes related to the movement
speed (see section 3.5.5.3). The relation between print order, surface quality and layer
interfaces should also be investigated.
Secondly, tests should give estimates on optimal slicing parameters that are related to
material properties. These include layer thickness, infill densities, limitations on slicing
angles and print head orientation, as well as allowable overhang dimensions.
86
==PAGE:97==
7
Conclusion
This thesis has focused on the development of a slicing and control application that can be
used to automatically create controlled movement of a robotic arm for the use in multi­axis
additive manufacturing, based on 3D objects representing relevant structures in concrete
AM. This has the potential of removing the necessity of support material for overhanging
structures, thus reducing material amounts used and environmental effects.
This aim has been undertaken by firstly reviewing the available literature on additive man­
ufacturing, with the focus on multi­axis slicing and printing approaches. This has resulted
in a review of current slicing methodologies and software, of which Cura has been found
the most relevant. Next, a range of potential slicing strategies, including improved conven­
tional slicing methods, discrete rotational, continuous rotational and non­planar strategies
have been investigated. For the type of structures expected in 3DCP, a continuous rota­
tional slicing approach has been deemed most relevant.
Secondly, a tool has been made to generate a print path from relevant 3D objects. This
tool has implemented an adapted continuous rotational slicing strategy, directed at sim­
ple structures, branching structures, arches and domes. The tool consists of a pre­slicing
stage, a slicing stage, followed by a post­slicing stage. The pre­slicing stage includes a
model decomposition step and the calculation of a model skeleton, which is used to de­
fine non­parallel slicing planes. These planes are subsequently used to slice the model,
resulting in a collection of line elements. These line elements are then processed to gen­
erate an accurate print path, potentially including additional infill. This print path is then
exported to G­code.
Thirdly, a tool has been created to translate this print path into controlled movement of the
robotic arm, suitable for 3D printing. This has been achieved by relating G­code to the
numerical control language used by the KUKA robotic arm.
Finally, the tools have been used to visualise potential print paths, using long­exposure
photography. This has shown an accurate creation of the print path from the tested 3D
models.
Comparisons have been made to compare the multi­rotational slicing approach to conven­
tional slicing methods. This has shown that there is a potential for significantly reducing
the material amounts in a printed structure. However, tests with the biopolymer concrete
should be performed before conclusions can be drawn from this potential.
Tests of the slicing and control applications with the concrete material should be done
to test the accuracy of the methods and to find suitable slicing parameters. Following
this, further developments of the slicing tool can be made, focusing at other slicing ap­
proaches, improved skeleton calculation, general script optimisation and extended post­
slicing steps.

D
List of potential errors
103
==PAGE:114==
Error message
Source
Probable explanation
Possible solution
ModuleNotFoundError: No module named ’xx’
Python
The required module is not installed.
Install the module
Error: input model not watertight
preslicing.check_watertight
The input model has faulty facets
Regenerate the STL file
Error: complete model below z=0 plane
preslicing.check_on_bottom
The model is properly not placed on the floor
Move the model vertically
Warning: model extents below z=0 plane
preslicing.check_on_bottom
The model is properly not placed on the floor
Move the model vertically
Error: complete model above z=0 plane
preslicing.check_on_bottom
The model is properly not placed on the floor
Move the model vertically
error in connecting faces
calc_laplacian
The laplacian cannot be calculated due to
a missing face
Regenerate the STL file
Error: element type unknown
# %% model partitioning
The model elements after arch partitioning
are defined incorrectly.
Check arch partitioning calculation
Error: model type unknown
# %% model partitioning
The parameter model_type is set incorrectly
Set model_type to any of the allowed options
Error in finding correct skeleton for
redefining partitioning planes
preslicing.redefine_boundary_planes_skel
There is an error in the classification of model
parts.
Check model parts classification, check
boundary planes
Error in slicing with plane: only one point (no line)
slicing.slicer
Slicing plane does not cross model properly
Check slicing plane definition, model
partitioning, layer height
Error in transforming slice to xy­plane
postslicing.slice_to_xy or
postslicing.lines_to_xy
The slice angles do not correspond properly to
the slice plane
Check slicing plane angles and angle limits
Error in infill generation with inner contours
postslicing.infill_zigzag_inner
There is a problem with intersecting the infill
lines with inner contours
Check infill generation and contours
Error: zigzag infill not possible for domes
postslicing.infill_zigzag_generate
Dome structures are not suitable for zigzag infill
Try W­infill
Error in infill generation: contour type unknown
postslicing.infill_zigzag_generate
The contour classification has resulted in an
unknown type
Check contours and classififcation
Error in W­infill, only works with one inner
and one outer contour
postslicing.W_infill
There are not just one inner and one outer
contour present
Check if model is suitable, check slicing
plane, check slice to contour splitting
error in intersection
tools.intersectiontest
There is a problem in calculating the intersection
between two lines
Check/plot possibly intersecting lines
ValueError: All­NaN slice encountered
preslicing.cut_model
The slicing planes do not slice the model
properly (results in no subvolumes)
Adjust partitioning parameters, check slicing
planes, check skeleton, check splines
slices[i­1][’normal’], slices[i­1][’origin’]) KeyError
Post­slicing
The previous slice number is not present,
probably removed (empty), possibly due to reversing spline
Check spline extrapolation, Manually remove
extra slices (either all before/after error slice)
AttributeError: ’NoneType’ object has no
attribute ’to_planar’
preslicing.cut_model
Trimesh is unable to cap the model
Check cutting plane, check spline
TypeError: m >k must hold
scipy.interpolate.splprep
The points to interpolate are not usable
Check spline points, most likely bottom_add
or top_add

load_STL_trimesh
15
def load_STL_trimesh(filename, scale=[1,1,1], move=[0,0,0], rotation=[0,0,0], remesh=0):
16
# load file
17
S = trimesh.load_mesh(filename)
18
19
# apply rotation
20
r = sp.spatial.transform.Rotation.from_euler('xyz', rotation, degrees=True)
21
S.vertices = r.apply(S.vertices)
22
23
# apply scaling
24
S = S.apply_scale(scale)
25
26
# apply translation
27
S = S.apply_translation(move)
28
29
# apply remesh
30
for i in range(remesh):
31
Sverts, Sfaces = trimesh.remesh.subdivide(S.vertices, S.faces)
32
S = trimesh.Trimesh(Sverts,Sfaces)
33
34
return S

def check_on_bottom(S):

53

54

55

57

61

print('z-min = ' + str(np.min(bounding_box[:,2])))

Listing F.13: check_on_bottom function
F.2.4
calc_laplacian
71
def calc_laplacian(S):
72
# calculate laplacian operator (Au2018 eq. 1)
73
74
#initialize
75
L = np.zeros((len(S.vertices),len(S.vertices)))
76
77
# defined for each vertex i
78
for i in range(len(S.vertices)):
79
# faces adjacent to i
80
adj_faces_i = S.vertex_faces[i]
81
adj_faces_i = adj_faces_i[adj_faces_i >=0]
82
adj_faces = S.faces[adj_faces_i]
83
84
# angles of faces adjacent to i
85
adj_angles = S.face_angles[adj_faces_i]
86
87
# vertices adjacent to i
88
adj_verts = S.vertex_neighbors[i]
89
90
# initialize sum part (for if i=j)
142
==PAGE:153==
91
l_sum = 0.
92
93
# calculate L[i,j]
94
for j in adj_verts:
95
# get faces adjacent to edje i-j (i.e. faces adjacent to i that also contain vertex j)
96
faces_i = np.any(j == adj_faces, axis=1)
97
98
# check if two faces adjacent to edge
99
if sum(faces_i) == 2:
100
# find angles of corners opposite to edge i-j
101
angle_i = np.all((adj_faces[faces_i] != i, adj_faces[faces_i] != j),axis=0)
102
angles = adj_angles[faces_i][angle_i]
103
104
# prevent devide by zero
105
if np.tan(angles[0]) == 0:
106
angles[0] = 1e-6
107
if np.tan(angles[1]) == 0:
108
angles[1] = 1e-6
109
110
# calculate Laplace element
111
L[i,j] = -1/2 * (1/np.tan(angles[0])+1/np.tan(angles[1]))
112
113
# add to sum for case i=j
114
l_sum = l_sum + (1/np.tan(angles[0])+1/np.tan(angles[1]))
115
else:
116
print('error in connecting faces')
117
print(i)
118
119
# sum over adjacent edges
120
L[i,i] = 1/2*l_sum
121
return L

def calc_ring_area(S):

126

128

contract_geometry
140
def contract_geometry(S, W_L, W_H, L):
141
# contract geometry based on Au2018
142
143
# get previous vertices
144
V = S.vertices
145
146
# update weighting matrices
147
WLL = np.dot(W_L,L)
148
WHV = np.dot(W_H,V)
149
O = np.zeros(V.shape)
150
151
# stack matrices
152
a = np.vstack((WLL,W_H))
153
b = np.vstack((O,WHV))
154
155
# solve vertex position system (Au2018 eq. 2)
156
V_new = sp.linalg.lstsq(a,b)
157
S.vertices = V_new[0]
158
159
return S

def calculate_skeleton(S, vollim = 10, w_L=1e-3, w_H = 1.0, s_L = 2.0):

fit_spline
459
def fit_spline(points, bottom_add = [], top_add = [], margin = 5):
460
# start with all points
461
p = points[:]
462
463
# remove identical
151
==PAGE:162==
464
p = np.unique(p,axis=0)
465
466
# sort vertically
467
p = p[p[:,2].argsort()]
468
469
# only keep points inside margin (%)
470
if isinstance(margin,list):
471
margin_lower = margin[0]/100
472
margin_upper = 1 - margin[1]/100
473
else:
474
margin_lower = margin/100
475
margin_upper = 1 - margin/100
476
477
478
p = p[np.all((p[:,2]>np.min(p[:,2])+(np.max(p[:,2])-np.min(p[:,2]))*margin_lower , p[:,2]<np.min(p[:,2])+(np.max(p[:,2])-np.min(p[:,2]))
*margin_upper),axis=0),:]
479
480
# initialize weights (to weigh added points more heavily)
481
weights = np.ones(len(p) + len(bottom_add) + len(top_add))
482
483
for b in range(len(bottom_add)):
484
p = np.insert(p,0,bottom_add[b],axis=0)
485
weights[0+len(bottom_add)-(b+1)] = 100
486
487
for t in range(len(top_add)):
488
p = np.insert(p,len(p),top_add[t],axis=0)
489
weights[len(weights)-len(top_add)+t] = 100
490
491
# fit spline
492
493
px = p[:,0]
494
py = p[:,1]
495
pz = p[:,2]
496
497
spline,u = sp.interpolate.splprep([px,py,pz],k=3,w=weights)
498
499
return spline

define_boundary_planes_lstsq
678
def define_boundary_planes_lstsq(S,CG,kmeans):
679
# define boundary planes with least squares of centroids of faces that connect model parts
680
681
# initialize
682
boundary_planes = []
683
684
# for each boundary as defined by the connectivity graph
685
for boundary in CG.edges:
686
687
## calculate vertices on boundary
688
# faces in k
689
faces_in_k = np.ones(S.faces.shape)*np.nan
690
faces_in_k[kmeans.labels_ == int(boundary[0]),:] = S.faces[kmeans.labels_ == int(boundary[0])]
691
# vertices in ki
692
verts_in_ki = np.unique(S.faces[kmeans.labels_ == int(boundary[1])].flatten())
693
# find faces in k that have vertices also in ki
694
_,ind,_ = np.intersect1d(faces_in_k.flatten(), verts_in_ki , return_indices=True)
695
faces_ind = np.unravel_index(ind, faces_in_k.shape)[0]
696
697
# find coordinates of centroids of faces connecting model parts
698
faces_centroids = np.mean(S.vertices[S.faces],axis=1)
699
boundary_coor = faces_centroids[np.unique(faces_ind)]
700
mean_boundary_coor = np.mean(boundary_coor ,axis=0)
701
702
# fit plane through centroids
703
C,_,_,_ = sp.linalg.lstsq(np.transpose(np.array([boundary_coor[:,0],boundary_coor[:,1], np.ones(boundary_coor.shape[0])])),
boundary_coor[:,2])
704
705
# store constants
706
plane_origin = np.array(mean_boundary_coor)
157
==PAGE:168==
707
plane_normal = np.array([C[0],C[1],-1])
708
plane_normal = plane_normal/np.linalg.norm(plane_normal)
709
710
# orient normal to boundary plane
711
if np.dot(plane_normal ,kmeans.cluster_centers_[int(boundary[0])]) - np.dot(plane_origin ,plane_normal) > 0:
712
plane_normal = -plane_normal
713
714
boundary_planes.append(np.array([boundary,plane_normal ,plane_origin]))
715
716
return np.array(boundary_planes)

def remove_cap(S,plane, distlim = 0.1):

def slicer(S,normal,origin):
13
14
plane_D = -np.dot(normal,origin)
15
16
outPlist = []
17
18
for face in S.faces:
19
outP = []
20
21
vertices = S.vertices[face]
22
23

25

26
27

29

31

35

36
t = d0 / (d0-d2)
37
outP.append(vertices[0,:] + t * (vertices[2,:]-vertices[0,:]))
38
39
if len(outP) > 0:
40
if len(outP) == 2:
41
outPlist.append(np.array(outP))
42
else:
43
print('Error in slicing with plane: only one point (no line)')
44
print('Try to re-partition model/nodes')
45
print(outP)
46
47
return np.array(outPlist)

def sort_lines(slice_lines_xy):

==PAGE:190==
89

def calc_lines_thickness(path,prev_normal ,prev_origin):
155
# calculate thickness
156
157
# calcuate extrusion based on previous layer orientation
158
SLC = np.mean(path,axis=1) # slice line center
159
DLT = (np.dot(SLC,prev_normal) - np.dot(prev_origin ,prev_normal))/np.linalg.norm(prev_normal)
160
161
return DLT

174
def CW_CCW(contour, slice_lines):
175
# align outer contours counterclockwise and inner contours clockwise
176
177
# initialize
178
direction_fixed = False
179
check_line = 0
180
181
# test until conclusive result
182
while direction_fixed == False and check_line<len(contour):
183

184
185
# count intersections with ray and slice on both sides of contour line, check if same result
186
for direction in [-1,1]:
187
contour_line = contour[check_line]
188

189
190
# ray tracing
191
u = contour_line[1]-contour_line[0]
182
==PAGE:193==
192
k = np.array([[0,0,0],[0,0,1]])
193
r = np.cross(u,k)
194
195
# extend ray (1e9)
196
perp_vect = direction*r*1e2 + midpoint
197
198
# count intersections with ray and whole slice
199
intersect = 0
200
for c_line in slice_lines:
201
if not np.any([np.all(c_line==contour_line),np.all(c_line==np.flip(contour_line ,axis=0))]):
202
if tools.intersectiontest(c_line,perp_vect)[1] == 1:
203
intersect = intersect + 1
204
change_dir.append(intersect)
205
206
# check if conclusive result
207
if np.mod(change_dir[0],2) == np.mod(change_dir[1],2):

# inconclusive , test different line
209
check_line += 1
210
else:
211
# conclusive , set orientation
212
if np.mod(intersect ,2) == 0:
213
pass
214
elif np.mod(intersect ,2) == 1:
215
contour = np.flip(contour,axis=[0,1])
216
217
# end loop
218
direction_fixed = True
219
220
return contour

def find_plot_scale(S):

30
z_scale = [z_lim[0] - z_add, z_lim[1] + z_add]
31
32

angles_to_ABC
9
def angles_to_ABC(angles, print_angle_lim):
10
(alpha,beta) = angles
11
alpha = np.degrees(alpha)
12
beta = np.degrees(beta)
13
beta = np.max((beta,-print_angle_lim))
14
15
A =
-alpha # +90.
16
B = -90. - beta
17
C =
90. - A
18
19
A = np.mod(A+180, 360) -180
20
B = np.mod(B+180, 360) -180
21
C = np.mod(C+180, 360) -180
22
23
if np.abs(beta) < 0.1: # near vertical
24
A = 0.
25
B = -90.
26
C = 90.
27
28
return (A, B, C)

angle_finder
10
def angle_finder(plane_n):
11

12
13

14

29
alpha = 0.

Listing F.82: angle_finder function
F.7.2
evaluate_spline
34
def evaluate_spline(spline, t, extend=0):
35
# evaluate spline length
36
du = 1e-3
37
u_eval = np.arange(0-extend ,1+extend,du)
38
[dx,dy,dz] = sp.interpolate.splev(u_eval,spline,der=1)
39
ds = np.sqrt(dx**2 + dy**2 + dz**2) * du
40
su = np.cumsum(ds)
41
spline_length = su[-1]
42
# Su = sp.interpolate.splev(u_eval,spline)
43
44
# calculate number of slices
45
maxNums = int(np.floor(1/t*su[-1]))
46
47
# define slicing planes
206
==PAGE:217==
48
points = np.zeros((maxNums ,3))
49
derivative = np.zeros((maxNums ,3))
50
51
for i in range(maxNums):
52
u_p = np.argmin(np.abs(su-i*t))
53
points[i,:] = sp.interpolate.splev(u_eval[u_p],spline)
54
derivative[i,:] = sp.interpolate.splev(u_eval[u_p],spline,der=1)
55
56
# slice horizontally at floor
57
if points[i,2] < 1e-1:
58
derivative[i,:] = np.array([0,0,1])
59
60
return points, derivative, maxNums, spline_length
Listing F.83: evaluate_spline function
F.7.3
intersectiontest
62
def intersectiontest(l1,l2):
63
64
p = l1[0][0:2]
65
r = l1[1][0:2] - p
66
67
q = l2[0][0:2]
68
s = l2[1][0:2] - q
69
70
if np.cross(r,s) == 0:
71
if np.cross((q-p),r) == 0:
72
intersectiontype = 2
73
else:
74
intersectiontype = 0
75
intersection = np.array((np.nan,np.nan))
76
else:
77
78
u = np.cross((q-p),r)/(np.cross(r,s))
79
t = np.cross((q-p),s)/(np.cross(r,s))

def translate_circ(gcode,last_movement):
197
for i in gcode:
198
if i[0] == 'X':
199
X = np.round(float(str(i[1:len(i)]))*scale_coor ,3)
200
elif i[0] == 'Y':
201
Y = np.round(float(str(i[1:len(i)]))*scale_coor ,3)
202
elif i[0] == 'I':
203
I = np.round(float(str(i[1:len(i)]))*scale_coor ,3)
204
elif i[0] == 'J':
205
J = np.round(float(str(i[1:len(i)]))*scale_coor ,3)
206
elif i[0] == 'Z':
207
print('error: vertical arcs not programmed')
208
209
for i in np.char.split(last_movement[1][1:len(last_movement[1])-1],', ').ravel()[0]:
210
if i[0] == 'X':
211
X_prev = np.round(float(str(i[2:len(i)]))*scale_coor ,3)
212
elif i[0] == 'Y':
213
Y_prev = np.round(float(str(i[2:len(i)]))*scale_coor ,3)
214
215
X_cen = X_prev+I
216
Y_cen = Y_prev+J
217
218
radius1 = np.sqrt((X_prev-X_cen)**2 + (Y_prev-Y_cen)**2)
219
radius2 = np.sqrt((X-X_cen)**2 + (Y-Y_cen)**2)
220
221
if np.round(radius1 ,1) != np.round(radius2 ,1):
222
print('error: arc not possible')
223
224
angle1 = np.arctan2(Y_prev-Y_cen,X_prev-X_cen)
225
angle2 = np.arctan2(Y-Y_cen,X-X_cen)
226
arc_angle = 2*np.pi - (angle2-angle1)
227
228
r1_angle = np.arctan((Y_cen-Y_prev)/(X_cen-X_prev))
229
hor_angle = arc_angle/2-r1_angle
230
231
X_delta = np.cos(hor_angle)*radius1
232
Y_delta = np.sin(hor_angle)*radius1
233
234
if gcode[0] == 'G2':
235
#clockwise
236
if arc_angle < np.pi:
213
==PAGE:224==
237
X_aux = X_cen - X_delta
238
Y_aux = Y_cen + Y_delta
239
else:
240
X_aux = X_cen + X_delta
241
Y_aux = Y_cen - Y_delta
242
243
elif gcode[0] == 'G3':
244
#counterclockwise
245
if arc_angle < np.pi:
246
X_aux = X_cen + X_delta
247
Y_aux = Y_cen - Y_delta
248
else:
249
X_aux = X_cen - X_delta
250
Y_aux = Y_cen + Y_delta
251

KRL_circ = '{X ' + str(np.round(X_aux ,3)) + ', Y ' + str(np.round(Y_aux ,3)) + '},{X
' + str(X) + ', Y ' + str(Y) + '}'
253
254
return KRL_circ
